جمیل احمد عفی عنہ
Translation: If someone bought a house, room, or dwelling in a house, the buyer will not have a pathway unless he buys it with every right that is established for it, or with its appurtenances (marāfiq), or with everything great and small. The same rule applies to water share and water channel because these are outside the boundaries [of the sold item] unless they are among the appendages (tawābi‘). So by mentioning the appendages, they will be included. Contrary to lease (ijārah), because lease is contracted to obtain benefit, and benefit cannot be obtained except with the pathway or water share and channel, because the lessee ordinarily does not buy the pathway nor does he take it on lease, so it will be included in the lease to obtain the desired benefit. But in sale, obtaining benefit from the sold item without a pathway is possible because the buyer buys the house and then trades it—that is, sells it to another—thus he obtains benefit. Explanation of the ruling: If a person bought a room, house, or dwelling within a house, there will be no pathway for the buyer. Yes, if the buyer mentions one of the three phrases—for example, buying a room or house or dwelling 'with every right that is established for it,' or 'with its appurtenances,' or 'with everything great and small'—then the pathway will be included in the sale and will be established for the buyer. Similarly, in the sale of land, the water share and water channel will not be included; meaning, if someone bought land and did not mention the right of water share and right of drainage, and did not mention any of the three phrases, then the buyer will have neither right of water share nor right of drainage. And if he mentions any of the three phrases, then the right of water share and drainage will be included in the sale. The proof is that the pathway, water share, and drainage are outside the boundaries of the sold item, but they are among the appendages of the sold item. So since these three things are outside the boundaries of the sold item, they will not be included in the sale without mentioning the appendages (the three phrases). But since they are among the appendages of the sale, they will be included by mentioning any of the three phrases. Contrary to lease: if a house is rented, the pathway will be included in the lease even if rights and appurtenances are not mentioned. Similarly, in the lease of land, the right of water share and drainage will be included even if none of the three phrases (i.e., rights or appurtenances) are mentioned. The reason is that lease is contracted to obtain benefit. For this reason, something that is not capable of yielding benefit immediately—like barren and saline land—its lease is not valid. So since lease is contracted to obtain benefit, and benefit from a house without a pathway and from land without water share and drainage is impossible, therefore, because the lessee ordinarily does not buy the pathway nor take it on lease, the pathway is included in the lease of a house, and the water share and drainage are included in the lease of land to obtain the desired benefit. In the case of sale, benefit from the sold item—the house without a pathway and the land without water share and drainage—can be obtained, and since benefit can be obtained without them, these things will not be included in the sale without mentioning the appendages (rights and appurtenances). And Allah knows best what is correct. Jameel Ahmad, may Allah forgive him.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage is a comparative ruling between sale (bay‘) and lease (ijārah) regarding the inclusion of ancillary rights like pathway (ṭarīq), water share (shurb), and drainage channel (masīl). The author first states the rule for sale: when a person buys a room, house, or dwelling within a larger property, the buyer does not get a right of way automatically. And if someone buys land, the right to water and drainage is not automatically included. For these rights to transfer, the buyer must explicitly mention them in the contract using one of three standard phrases: (1) 'with every right that is established for it' (bi-kulli ḥaqq thābit lahu), (2) 'with its appurtenances' (bi-marāfiqihi), or (3) 'with everything great and small' (bi-kulli qalīl wa kathīr). These three phrases are called the 'three expressions' (ʿibārāt thalāthah). The proof (dalīl) is that the pathway, water share, and drainage are outside the physical boundaries (ḥudūd) of the sold item, but they are regarded as appendages (tawābi‘) to it. Since they are external, they are not included by default; since they are appendages, they become included when the appendages are mentioned. In contrast, for lease (ijārah), these rights are automatically included without any such mention. The reason (illah) is that the purpose of a lease is to obtain benefit (manfa‘ah), and benefit from a house or land is impossible without a pathway and water/drainage rights. The lessee (musta’jir) does not buy these rights separately or take them on lease; they are inherently necessary for the use of the leased property. Therefore, they are subsumed under the lease contract by necessity. In sale, however, the purpose is not necessarily immediate use; the buyer may resell the property or use it in another way that does not require these rights. Thus, the ruling is different. This is a clear example of the Hanafi principle that contracts are interpreted according to their purposes and customs.

Key Terms
مرافق (marāfiq): Appurtenances or ancillary rights that are necessary for the use of the property but are not part of its physical boundaries.
توابع (tawābi‘): Things that follow the main object as appendages; they are not part of the boundaries but are legally attached to the main object.
عبارات ثلاثہ (ʿibārāt thalāthah): The three specific phrases that, when used in a sale contract, include all ancillary rights: 'with every right,' 'with its appurtenances,' 'with everything great and small.'

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a sale contract, under what condition are rights like pathway and water share included without explicit mention?
A) They are always included automatically.
B) They are included if the buyer intends to use the property himself.
C) They are included only if the seller agrees verbally.
D) They are not included automatically; only if one of the three standard phrases is used.

Key Takeaway
Ancillary rights (pathway, water share, drainage) are not automatically included in sale unless explicitly mentioned via the three phrases, but are automatically included in lease due to the contract's purpose of benefit.

Bayyinah vs. Iqrar in Claiming a Slave-Girl and Child
Distinguishes the effect of proof (bayyinah) from acknowledgment (iqrar) in claiming a slave-girl and her child

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether a child follows the mother when ownership is proven by bayyinah versus iqrar, and explain the legal reasoning (wajh al-farq) based on the classification of proofs.

Core Idea
Bayyinah proves ownership from the origin, including the child; iqrar only establishes ownership from the moment of acknowledgment, so the child is excluded.

When a third party claims ownership of a slave-girl who has given birth, the outcome depends on how the claim is proven. If the claimant produces witnesses (bayyinah), both mother and child are returned to him. If the buyer merely acknowledges (iqrar) the claimant's right, only the mother is given. This lesson unpacks the difference by analyzing the nature of each proof: bayyinah is a comprehensive proof that uncovers pre-existing ownership, while iqrar is a limited proof that only validates the statement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Read the passage and note the two distinct rulings: when the claimant comes with bayyinah, he takes both the slave-girl and her child. When the buyer makes iqrar for someone, that person takes only the slave-girl, not the child.
2
Understand the legal nature of bayyinah
Bayyinah is defined as a 'comprehensive proof' (ḥujjah muṭlaqah). It clarifies and reveals ownership from the beginning (min al-aṣl).
The term bayyinah itself implies clarification (bayan).
3
Understand the legal nature of iqrar
Iqrar is a 'limited proof' (ḥujjah qāṣirah). It establishes ownership only to the extent necessary to validate the statement (ikhbār), and only from the moment of acknowledgment, not retroactively.
Iqrar is based on necessity: you cannot call a person a liar who acknowledges a right against himself.
4
Apply the reasoning to the child
Since bayyinah proves ownership from the origin, the child, who was conceived and born while the mother was owned by the claimant from the start, is included. With iqrar, the child was born before the acknowledgment, so the necessity of validating the iqrar is fulfilled by giving the mother alone; the child is a separate entity.
The child's connection (ittiṣāl) to the mother at the time of origin is key.
5
Note the judicial condition
According to Imam Muhammad, even with bayyinah, if the judge is unaware of the child (or the child is in another's possession), the child is not automatically included in the ruling. This adds a practical nuance to the general rule.
This is an intra-Hanafi variation mentioned in the Masā’il Mabsūṭ.

Source Text

تشریح استحقاق حق طلب کرنا ۔ اس باب اور باب الحقوق میں مناسبت ظاہر ہے۔
باندی خریدی اس نے مشتری کے پاس بچہ دے دیا ایک مستحق بینہ کے ساتھ نکل آیا وہ باندی اور اس کے بچہ کو لے سکتا ہے اگر مشتری نے کسی کیلئے اقرار کر لیا تو بچہ نہیں شامل ہوگا
كتاب البيوع
ومن اشترى جارية فولدت عنده فاستحقها رجل فانه ياخذها وولدها وان اقر بها لرجل لم يتبعها ولدها ووجه الفرق ان البيئة حجة مطلقة فانها كاسمها مبينة فيظهر بها ملكه من الاصل والولد كان متصلا بها فيكون له اما الاقرار حجة قاصرة يثبت الملك فى المخبر به ضرورة صحة الاخبار وقد اندفعت باثباته بعد الانفصال فلا يكون الولد له ثم قيل يدخل الولد فى القضاء بالام تبعا وقيل يشترط القضاء بالولد واليه تشير المسائل فان القاضى اذا لم يعلم بالزوائد قال محمد لا تدخل الزوائد فى الحكم وكذا الولد اذا كان في يد غيره لا يدخل تحت الحكم بالام تبعا
ترجمہ اور اگر کسی نے ایک باندی خریدی پھر اس نے مشتری کے پاس بچہ جنا پھر ایک آدمی گواہوں کے ذریعہ اس کا مستحق ہو گیا تو مستحق باندی اور اس کے بچہ کو لے لے گا۔ اور اگر مشتری نے کسی آدمی کیلئے اس باندی کا اقرار کر لیا تو باندی کا بچہ اس کے پیچھے نہیں جائے گا اور وجہ فرق یہ ہے کہ بینہ حجت مطلقہ ہے اسلئے کہ وہ اپنے نام کے مانند ظاہر کرنے والا ہے ، پس بینہ کے ذریعہ اس کی ملک اصل سے ظاہر ہو گی اور بچہ (چونکہ ) اس کے ساتھ متصل تھا اسلئے بچہ اسی کا ہوگا۔ اور رہا اقرار تو وہ حجت قاصرہ ہے اخبار صحیح ہونے کی ضرورت سے مخبر بہ ( باندی ) میں ملک کو ثابت کرے گا اور یہ ضرورت بچہ جدا ہونے کے بعد ملک ثابت کرنے سے پوری ہو جاتی ہے تو بچہ اسکا نہ ہوگا۔ پھر کہا گیا کہ بچہ کے واسطے حکم قضاء کا ہونا شرط ہے اور اسی قول کی جانب مسائل مبسوط اشارہ کرتے ہیں ۔ چنانچہ قاضی کو اگر زوائد کا عمل نہ ہو تو امام محمد نے کہا کہ زوائد حکم قضاء میں داخل نہ ہوں گے۔ اور اسی طرح بچہ اگر دوسرے کے قبضہ میں ہو تو ماں کے تابع ہو کر حکم قضاء کے تحت داخل نہیں ہو گا۔
تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ ایک شخص نے باندی خرید کر ا پر قبضہ کر لیا پھر باندی نے مشتری کے پاس اپنے مولی کے علاوہ کسی دوسرے کے نطفہ سے بچہ جنا۔ پھر ایک آدمی نے اس باندی پر گواہوں کے ذریعہ اپنا استحقاق ثابت کر دیا تو یہ مستحق باندی کو بھی لے لے گا اور اسکے ساتھ اس کے بچہ کو بھی لے لے گا۔ اور اگر مشتری نے کسی آدمی کیلئے اس باندی کا اقرار کیا تو مقرلہ باندی کو تو لے لے گا لیکن اسکے بچہ کونہیں لے گا۔ یعنی جس کے واسطے مشتری نے باندی کا اقرار کیا ہے اس کو باندی تو دے دی جائے گی لیکن اس کا بچہ نہیں دیا جائے گا۔
Chapter of Claim (Istiḥqāq). Translation: This chapter is about claim. Explanation: Istiḥqāq means demanding a right. The appropriateness between this chapter and the chapter on rights is obvious. A slave-girl was bought, she gave birth at the buyer's place, a claimant came with evidence (bayyinah) — he may take the slave-girl and her child. If the buyer made an acknowledgment (iqrār) for someone, the child will not be included. Kitab al-Buyu': And whoever bought a slave-girl and she gave birth while in his possession, then a man claimed her (by evidence), he takes her and her child. And if he acknowledged her for a man, her child does not follow her. The difference is that bayyinah is a comprehensive proof (ḥujjah muṭlaqah) because it is, as its name implies, clarifying; so through it his ownership is shown from the origin, and the child was connected to her, so it belongs to him. As for iqrār, it is a limited proof (ḥujjah qāṣirah) that establishes ownership in the thing acknowledged only out of necessity to validate the report (ikhbār), and this necessity is fulfilled by establishing ownership after separation, so the child is not his. Then it was said that the child's inclusion requires a judicial decree (qaḍā'). The expositions (Masā’il Mabsūṭ) allude to this view. So if the judge is not aware of the accessories (zawā’id), Imam Muhammad said that accessories are not included in the ruling. Similarly, if the child is in the possession of another, it does not come under the ruling as a dependent of the mother. Explanation of the case: A person bought a slave-girl and took possession. Then the slave-girl gave birth to a child from the seed of someone other than her owner, while with the buyer. Then a man established his claim over the slave-girl by witnesses. This claimant will take both the slave-girl and her child. And if the buyer acknowledged the slave-girl for someone, the acknowledged person will take the slave-girl but not her child. That is, the person for whom the buyer acknowledged the slave-girl will be given the slave-girl, but her child will not be given to him.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning the difference between proving ownership through evidence (bayyinah) and through acknowledgment (iqrār) in the context of a claimed slave-girl who has given birth. The ruling presents itself in two scenarios: (1) If a person bought a slave-girl, she gives birth while in his possession, and then a third party proves his ownership of the slave-girl by presenting witnesses (bayyinah), the successful claimant takes both the slave-girl and her child. (2) If the buyer merely acknowledges (iqrār) that the slave-girl belongs to someone else (without evidence from the claimant), then that person takes the slave-girl but not the child. The author then explains the legal reasoning (wajh al-farq): bayyinah (evidence) is a 'comprehensive proof' (ḥujjah muṭlaqah) because it reveals what is true and establishes the claimant's ownership from the beginning (min al-aṣl). Since the child was physically connected to the mother at the time of conception and birth, and the mother belonged to the claimant from the start, the child also belongs to him. In contrast, iqrār is a 'limited proof' (ḥujjah qāṣirah). It establishes ownership only to the extent necessary to validate the acknowledgment itself; the ownership is established only from the moment of iqrār, not retroactively. Since the child was born and separated from the mother before the iqrār, the necessity of validating the iqrār is satisfied by giving the mother alone. The child is an independent entity after birth, so the iqrār does not extend to him. The passage also mentions an additional condition: some scholars say that for the child to be included in the ruling, the judge must explicitly include the child in the decree. If the judge is unaware of the child (accessories), or if the child is in the possession of another person, the child does not automatically fall under the ruling as a dependent of the mother. This is the view of Imam Muhammad, as indicated in the Masā’il Mabsūṭ. The commentary further clarifies the scenario: the buyer had possession, and the child was conceived from someone else's sperm (not the buyer's), so the child is not the buyer's. The claimant establishes his ownership over the slave-girl through witnesses. The ruling follows the established Hanafi principle that evidence proves pre-existing title; acknowledgment establishes title only prospectively.

Key Terms
استحقاق (istiḥqāq): A claim of right or entitlement, specifically where a third party proves ownership of an object in the possession of another.
بینه (bayyinah): Clear evidence or testimony that establishes a fact; specifically, proof that reveals ownership from the beginning.
اقرار (iqrār): A unilateral acknowledgment by a person in possession that the property belongs to another; it establishes ownership only to validate the acknowledgment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does evidence (bayyinah) include the child of a claimed slave-girl, but acknowledgment (iqrar) does not?
A) Because bayyinah is from witnesses and iqrar is from the buyer.
B) Because bayyinah proves ownership from the origin, so the child (connected) belongs; iqrar establishes ownership only from the moment of acknowledgment, and the child was separated before that.
C) Because the child can only be claimed separately with separate evidence.
D) Because iqrar is only valid for movable property, not children.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes bayyinah as comprehensive proof that reveals pre-existing ownership, thus including the child, while iqrar is limited proof that only establishes ownership from the moment of acknowledgment, thereby excluding the child.

Bayyinah vs Iqrar: Scope of Proof in Ownership Claims
Explains distinction between bayyinah and iqrar when a purchased slave-girl is claimed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between bayyinah and iqrar in ownership claims and state why the child follows in one case but not the other.

Core Idea
Bayyinah reveals ownership from the origin, so the child is included; iqrar only proves ownership after separation, so the child is not.

In the Book of Sales, al-Hidayah presents a nuanced ruling: when a purchased slave-girl is claimed, the outcome depends on how the claim is proven. If the claimant produces bayyinah (clear evidence), both mother and child return to him. But if the purchaser merely acknowledges (iqrar) that the slave-girl belongs to another, only the mother is transferred. This difference stems from the nature of each proof: bayyinah is absolute, iqrar is limited. Let us explore the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage contrasts two cases: (1) claimant produces bayyinah, (2) purchaser makes iqrar. State the ruling for each: with bayyinah, claimant takes both mother and child; with iqrar, only mother.
2
Understand the nature of each proof
Bayyinah is described as hujjah mutlaqah (absolute proof) that reveals original ownership from the origin. Iqrar is hujjah qasirah (limited proof) that establishes ownership only in the acknowledged item at the time of acknowledgment, out of necessity for the validity of the report.
The key distinction: bayyinah shows ownership existed before; iqrar creates ownership at the moment of admission.
3
Apply the nature to the child
Because bayyinah proves ownership from the origin, and the child was physically connected to the mother at that time, the child belongs to the claimant. With iqrar, ownership is established after separation, so the child is not included.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
Some Hanafi scholars say the child enters the judge's ruling automatically with the mother. Others, including Imam Muhammad, require a separate judgment for the child. The relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is the latter, as indicated by the issues in al-Mabsut.
Imam Muhammad's view is from the zahir al-riwayah.
5
Determine the practical consequence
If the judge is unaware of the child, or if the child is in another's possession, it is not automatically awarded with the mother. A separate judgment is needed to ensure clarity and avoid legal complications.

تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ ایک شخص نے باندی خرید کر ا پر قبضہ کر لیا پھر باندی نے مشتری کے پاس اپنے مولی کے علاوہ کسی دوسرے کے نطفہ سے بچہ جنا۔ پھر ایک آدمی نے اس باندی پر گواہوں کے ذریعہ اپنا استحقاق ثابت کر دیا تو یہ مستحق باندی کو بھی لے لے گا اور اسکے ساتھ اس کے بچہ کو بھی لے لے گا۔ اور اگر مشتری نے کسی آدمی کیلئے اس باندی کا اقرار کیا تو مقرلہ باندی کو تو لے لے گا لیکن اسکے بچہ کونہیں لے گا۔ یعنی جس کے واسطے مشتری نے باندی کا اقرار کیا ہے اس کو باندی تو دے دی جائے گی لیکن اس کا بچہ نہیں دیا جائے گا۔
بینہ اور اقرار کے درمیان وجہ فرق یہ ہے کہ بینہ تمام لوگوں کے حق میں حجت کاملہ ہے۔ اسلئے کہ بعینہ قضاء قاضی کی وجہ سے حجت ہوتا ہے اور قاضی کو ولایت عامہ حاصل ہے۔ اسلئے بعینہ تمام لوگوں کے حق میں حجت کاملہ ہوگا اور بعینہ جیسا کہ اس کے نام سے ظاہر ہے اصلی حالت کو ظاہر کرنے والا ہے۔ یعنی جو چیز نفس الامر میں شہادت سے پہلے ثابت تھی۔ بعینہ اس کو ظاہر کر دیتا ہے۔ پس بینہ کے ذریعہ رجل مستحق کی ملک اصل سے ثابت ہوگی یعنی بینہ کے ذریعہ یہ ثابت ہو جائے گا یہ باندی اصلا اسی کی ملک ہے اور اس وقت چونکہ بچہ بھی باندی کے ساتھ متصل تھا۔ اسلئے جب باندی رجل مستحق کی ملک ہوئی تو اس کا بچہ بھی اسی رجل مستحق کا ہوگا۔
اور رہا اقرار تو وہ ناقص حجت ہے فقط اقرار صحیح ہونے کی ضرورت سے باندی میں فی الحال ملکیت ثابت ہو جائے گی۔ اور یہ ضرورت بچہ پیدا ہونے کے بعد صرف باندی میں ملکیت ثابت ہونے سے پوری ہو جاتی ہے۔ تو گویا مشتری کے اقرار کرنے کی وجہ سے مقرل کی ملکیت صرف باندی میں ثابت ہوئی۔ اور بچہ میں ثابت نہیں ہوئی۔ پس جب مقررہ کی ملکیت باندی میں ثابت ہوئی اور بچہ میں ثابت نہیں ہوئی تو مقرلۂ فقط باندی لینے کا مجاز ہوگا اور بچہ لینے کا مجاز نہ ہوگا۔
ثم قيل يدخل الولد ... الخ سے ایک اختلاف کی طرف اشارہ ہے ۔ اختلاف یہ ہے کہ مدعی کے بینہ پیش کرنے کے بعد جب قاضی نے باندی کے بارے میں فیصلہ دیا کہ یہ باندی مدعی یعنی مستحق کی ہے تو کیا اس کا بچہ اپنی ماں کے ساتھ تابع ہو کر حکم قضاء میں داخل ہوگا یا بچہ کے واسطے علیحدہ سے فیصلہ صادر کرنا شرط ہے۔ سو بعض حضرات مشائخ کا قول یہ ہے کہ بچہ ماں کے ساتھ تابع ہو کر حکم قضاء میں داخل ہو جائے گا اسکے واسطے علیحدہ سے فیصلہ دینے کی ضروت نہیں ہے اور بعض حضرات مشائخ نے کہا ہے کہ بچہ کے واسطے مستقل حکم قضا شرط ہے اور اسی کی طرف مبسوط کے مسائل اشارہ کرتے ہیں۔ چنانچہ اگر قاضی کو زوائد کا علم نہ ہو تو امام محمد نے کہا کہ حکم قاضی میں زوائد داخل نہ ہوں گے۔ اسی طرح اگر بچہ مستحق کے علاوہ کسی دوسرے کے قبضہ میں ہو اور وہ غالب ہو اور قاضی نے مستحق کیلئے باندی کا فیصلہ خریدا ہو تو اس فیصلہ میں ماں کے تابع ہو کر بچہ داخل نہیں ہو گا۔ ان دونوں مسئلوں سے ظاہر ہوتا ہے کہ بچہ اپنی ماں کے تابع ہو کرحکم قضاء میں داخل نہیں ہوتا بلکہ بچہ کے واسطے مستقل حکم قاضی ضروری ہے۔
Chapter of Istiḥqāq: Translation: This chapter is about the explanation of claim. Explanation: Istiḥqāq means to demand a right. There is apparent suitability between this chapter and the chapter of Rights. A purchased slave-girl gave birth at the purchaser's place; a claimant appears with clear evidence; he can take the slave-girl and her child. If the purchaser made an acknowledgment for someone, the child will not be included. Book of Sales: Whoever buys a slave-girl and she gives birth in his possession, then a man claims her, he takes her and her child. If he acknowledges her for a man, her child does not follow her. The difference is that evidence is an absolute proof, for it is like its name: manifest. So by it his ownership from the origin is manifested, and the child was connected to her, so it is his. As for acknowledgment, it is a limited proof; it establishes ownership in the thing acknowledged out of necessity for the correctness of the report, and that necessity is fulfilled by establishing ownership after separation, so the child is not his. Then it is said that the child enters the judgment with the mother as a follow-up, and it is said that judgment on the child is required, and to this the issues point. For if the judge does not know of the increases, Muhammad said that the increases do not enter the judgment. Similarly, if the child is in the hand of another, it does not enter the judgment with the mother as a follow-up.
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah discusses the chapter of claim (istiḥqāq) in the Book of Sales. It presents a ruling with proof. The ruling distinguishes between two scenarios when a purchased slave-girl is claimed by a third party. In the first scenario, the claimant produces clear evidence (bayyinah). In the second, the purchaser makes an acknowledgment (iqrar) that the slave-girl belongs to someone else. The passage first states the ruling: with bayyinah, the claimant takes both the slave-girl and her child; with iqrar, the child does not follow the mother. The proof for the difference is the nature of the evidence. Bayyinah is an absolute proof (ḥujjah muṭlaqah) because it reveals the original ownership. Since the child was connected to the mother at the time of the claimed ownership, the child also belongs to the claimant. Iqrar is a limited proof (ḥujjah qāṣirah); it establishes ownership in the acknowledged item (the slave-girl) only out of necessity for the validity of the report. That necessity is satisfied by proving ownership of the mother after separation, so the child is not included. The passage then mentions a dispute (ikhtilāf) among Hanafi scholars: some say the child follows the mother in the judge's ruling without needing a separate judgment; others, including Imam Muhammad, say a separate judgment on the child is required. The commentary explains that if the judge is unaware of the child, or if the child is in someone else's possession, it is not included automatically. This shows that according to the relied-upon position, the child needs its own judgment.

Key Terms
بينة (bayyinah): Clear evidence, typically witness testimony, that proves ownership or a right from the origin.
إقرار (iqrar): An acknowledgment or admission by a person (here the purchaser) that a property belongs to another.
حجة مطلقة (hujjah mutlaqah): An absolute proof that establishes truth universally, not restricted to the parties.
حجة قاصرة (hujjah qasirah): A limited proof that only applies to the person who made the admission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the child not follow the mother when the purchaser makes an iqrar?
A) Because bayyinah is produced after the child is born.
B) Because iqrar is a limited proof that only establishes ownership after separation.
C) Because bayyinah requires two witnesses, while iqrar requires only one.
D) Because the child is always with the mother in all cases.

Key Takeaway
The scope of proof determines the scope of ownership: bayyinah is absolute and includes the child, iqrar is limited and covers only the acknowledged item.

Purchased Slave Found Free: Recourse and Liability
Ruling on recourse when purchased slave is free and the slave ordered the purchase

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on recourse when a purchased slave is found free, distinguish between the positions of Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf, and identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
Liability for a free slave depends on the buyer's reliance on his command and the seller's traceability.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a purchased slave who turns out to be free but had instructed the buyer to purchase him. The ruling turns on whether the buyer relied on the slave's statement and whether recourse against the seller is possible. We will trace the reasoning of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad versus Abu Yusuf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
Distinguish based on seller's presence: present/traceable, absent untraceable in purchase, and pledge.
The text divides into purchase (seller known vs unknown) and pledge.
2
State the majority view
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that in purchase, recourse is only against the slave when the seller is untraceable; in pledge, never.
3
State Abu Yusuf's view
He holds that the slave is liable in both cases because the false information creates a guarantee.
4
Analyse the dalil of each side
Understand the basis: reliance vs. false information as guarantee. The majority emphasize the buyer's reliance on the slave's command.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: in pledge, under majority no recourse; under Abu Yusuf, recourse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غلام خرید او آزاد نکلا حالانکہ غلام نے مشتری سے کہا کہ تم مجھے خریدلو میں فلاں کا غلام ہوں بائع حاضر ہو یا غا ئب ہو اور اس کا پتہ معلوم ہو تو غلام پر کچھ لازم نہ ہوگا ور نہ مشتر می غلام سے رجوع کرے گا
قال ومن اشترى عبدا فاذا هو حر وقد قال العبد للمشترى اشترنى فانى عبد له فان كان البائع حاضرا او غائبا غيبة معروفة لم يكن على العبد شيء وان كان البائع لا يدرى اين هو رجع المشترى على العبد ورجع هو على البائع. ان ارتهن عبدا مقرا بالعبودية فوجده حرا لم يرجع عليه على كل حال وعن أبي يوسف انه يرجع فيهما لان الرجوع بالمعاوضة او بالكفالة والموجود ليس الا الاخبار كاذبا فصار كما اذا قال الاجنبي ذالک اوقال العبد ارتهنى فانى عبد وهى المسالة الثانية ولهما ان المشترى شرع في الشراء معتمدا على امره واقراره الى عبد اذ القول له في الحرية فيجعل العبد بالأمر بالشراء ضامنا للثمن له عند تعذر رجوعه
A purchased slave appeared free, even though the slave had said to the purchaser: 'Buy me, for I am the slave of so-and-so.' If the seller is present or absent but his whereabouts known, nothing is due from the slave. If the seller's whereabouts are unknown, the purchaser has recourse against the slave, and the slave has recourse against the seller. If he took a slave as collateral who admitted to slavery, then found him free, he has no recourse in any case. And from Abu Yusuf: he has recourse in both cases, because recourse comes from exchange or guarantee, and what exists is only false information, so it becomes like when a stranger says that, or when the slave says 'Pledge me, for I am a slave' – that is the second issue. And for the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad): the purchaser began the purchase relying on his order and admission, and the slave's statement is accepted regarding freedom, so the slave becomes liable for the price by his order to purchase, when recourse to the seller is impossible.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage discusses a second ruling in the chapter of claim: a person buys a slave, but the slave turns out to be free. The slave had previously told the buyer: 'Buy me, for I am the slave of so-and-so' (implying he is a slave). The ruling distinguishes three scenarios based on the seller's availability. If the seller is present or absent but traceable, the buyer's recourse is against the seller, not the slave. If the seller's whereabouts are unknown, then the buyer can take recourse against the slave, and the slave then has recourse against the seller. The matn then adds: if a slave is taken as collateral (rahn) and he admits to being a slave, then is found free, the creditor has no recourse against the slave in any case (whether seller is traceable or not). A dispute (intra-Hanafi) follows: Abu Yusuf holds that recourse is possible in both cases (purchase and pledge) because the slave's false statement amounts to a guarantee. The majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) disagree: in the purchase case, the buyer relied on the slave's order and admission; since the slave's word is accepted regarding his own freedom (he could not himself be a slave), he becomes liable for the price when recourse to the seller is impossible. But in the pledge case, the transaction is not based on the slave's order to the creditor to accept him as collateral; rather the creditor acted on his own judgment, so no recourse.

Key Terms
رجوع (ruju'): Right of recourse to recover the price paid from the slave or seller.
ارتهن (irtahana): To take something as a pledge or collateral.
مقر بالعبودية (muqirr bil-'ubudiyyah): A person who admits to being a slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, when does a buyer have recourse against a purchased slave who turned out free and who told the buyer to buy him?
A) Always, because the slave lied.
B) Only when the seller is untraceable.
C) Only when the seller is present.
D) Never, because the buyer must verify the slave's status.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a free person who orders a buyer to purchase him becomes liable for the price only when the buyer relied on that order and the seller is untraceable; in pledge transactions, no such liability arises.

Bayyinah and Iqrar: Ownership of a Slave and Her Child
Intra-Hanafi dispute on automatic inclusion of child

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why bayyinah establishes full ownership including the child while iqrar does not, and distinguish the two positions in the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the child's inclusion.

Core Idea
Bayyinah reveals original ownership; iqrar only establishes present ownership for necessity.

This passage examines a crucial distinction between bayyinah (evidentiary proof) and iqrār (confession) in establishing ownership of a slave-girl and her child. Understanding this distinction is key to grasping how different legal proofs operate in Hanafi fiqh. The passage also highlights an intra-Hanafi debate about whether a judge's ruling on the mother automatically includes her child, with practical implications for property and possession.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two proofs and their nature
Note that bayyinah is described as a complete proof (hujjah kāmilah) for all people, while iqrār is an incomplete proof.
Bayyinah reveals the original state; iqrār only establishes present ownership for necessity.
2
Understand why bayyinah is complete
Because the judge has general authority (wilāyah āmmah), and bayyinah manifests what was true before the testimony. Thus, ownership is established from the origin.
The child was attached at that original time, so it follows the mother.
3
Understand why iqrar is incomplete
Iqrar only serves the necessity of making the confession valid; it establishes ownership of the slave-girl alone, not the child.
The necessity is fulfilled by proving present ownership of the mother only.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Some mashāyikh hold the child automatically enters the judge's ruling on the mother; others require a separate judgment. The passage cites Imām Muḥammad's counterexamples to support the latter view.
The muftā bihi position is that a separate judgment is needed, based on al-Mabsūṭ.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بینہ اور اقرار کے درمیان وجہ فرق یہ ہے کہ بینہ تمام لوگوں کے حق میں حجت کاملہ ہے۔ اسلئے کہ بعینہ قضاء قاضی کی وجہ سے حجت ہوتا ہے اور قاضی کو ولایت عامہ حاصل ہے۔ اسلئے بعینہ تمام لوگوں کے حق میں حجت کاملہ ہوگا اور بعینہ جیسا کہ اس کے نام سے ظاہر ہے اصلی حالت کو ظاہر کرنے والا ہے۔ یعنی جو چیز نفس الامر میں شہادت سے پہلے ثابت تھی۔ بعینہ اس کو ظاہر کر دیتا ہے۔ پس بینہ کے ذریعہ رجل مستحق کی ملک اصل سے ثابت ہوگی یعنی بینہ کے ذریعہ یہ ثابت ہو جائے گا یہ باندی اصلا اسی کی ملک ہے اور اس وقت چونکہ بچہ بھی باندی کے ساتھ متصل تھا۔ اسلئے جب باندی رجل مستحق کی ملک ہوئی تو اس کا بچہ بھی اسی رجل مستحق کا ہوگا۔ اور رہا اقرار تو وہ ناقص حجت ہے فقط اقرار صحیح ہونے کی ضرورت سے باندی میں فی الحال ملکیت ثابت ہو جائے گی۔ اور یہ ضرورت بچہ پیدا ہونے کے بعد صرف باندی میں ملکیت ثابت ہونے سے پوری ہو جاتی ہے۔ تو گویا مشتری کے اقرار کرنے کی وجہ سے مقرل کی ملکیت صرف باندی میں ثابت ہوئی۔ اور بچہ میں ثابت نہیں ہوئی۔ پس جب مقررہ کی ملکیت باندی میں ثابت ہوئی اور بچہ میں ثابت نہیں ہوئی تو مقرلۂ فقط باندی لینے کا مجاز ہوگا اور بچہ لینے کا مجاز نہ ہوگا۔ ثم قيل يدخل الولد ... الخ سے ایک اختلاف کی طرف اشارہ ہے ۔ اختلاف یہ ہے کہ مدعی کے بینہ پیش کرنے کے بعد جب قاضی نے باندی کے بارے میں فیصلہ دیا کہ یہ باندی مدعی یعنی مستحق کی ہے تو کیا اس کا بچہ اپنی ماں کے ساتھ تابع ہو کر حکم قضاء میں داخل ہوگا یا بچہ کے واسطے علیحدہ سے فیصلہ صادر کرنا شرط ہے۔ سو بعض حضرات مشائخ کا قول یہ ہے کہ بچہ ماں کے ساتھ تابع ہو کر حکم قضاء میں داخل ہو جائے گا اسکے واسطے علیحدہ سے فیصلہ دینے کی ضروت نہیں ہے اور بعض حضرات مشائخ نے کہا ہے کہ بچہ کے واسطے مستقل حکم قضا شرط ہے اور اسی کی طرف مبسوط کے مسائل اشارہ کرتے ہیں۔ چنانچہ اگر قاضی کو زوائد کا علم نہ ہو تو امام محمد نے کہا کہ حکم قاضی میں زوائد داخل نہ ہوں گے۔ اسی طرح اگر بچہ مستحق کے علاوہ کسی دوسرے کے قبضہ میں ہو اور وہ غالب ہو اور قاضی نے مستحق کیلئے باندی کا فیصلہ خریدا ہو تو اس فیصلہ میں ماں کے تابع ہو کر بچہ داخل نہیں ہو گا۔ ان دونوں مسئلوں سے ظاہر ہوتا ہے کہ بچہ اپنی ماں کے تابع ہو کرحکم قضاء میں داخل نہیں ہوتا بلکہ بچہ کے واسطے مستقل حکم قاضی ضروری ہے۔
The reason for the difference between evidence (bayyinah) and confession (iqrar) is that evidence is a complete proof for all people, because the very thing becomes a proof by the judgment of the judge, and the judge has general authority. Therefore, it becomes a complete proof for all people, and as its name indicates, it reveals the original state—that is, what was established in reality before the testimony. It manifests it. So through evidence, the ownership of the rightful man is established from the origin, i.e., through evidence it will be proven that this slave-girl is originally his property, and since the child was attached to the slave-girl at that time, when the slave-girl became the property of the rightful man, her child also becomes that man's. As for confession, it is an incomplete proof; only for the validity of the confession, ownership of the slave-girl is established at present. And this necessity is fulfilled after the birth of the child merely by establishing ownership of the slave-girl. So, due to the buyer's confession, the ownership of the confessor is established only in the slave-girl, not in the child. Thus, when the ownership of the confesee is established in the slave-girl but not in the child, the confesee is only entitled to take the slave-girl, not the child. Then the phrase 'thumma qīla yadkhulu al-walad...' indicates a difference of opinion. The dispute is: after the claimant produces evidence and the judge rules that the slave-girl belongs to the claimant, does her child enter the judgment as a dependent with his mother, or is a separate judgment for the child required? Some mashāyikh say the child enters the judgment as a dependent with the mother; no separate judgment is needed. Other mashāyikh say a separate judgment for the child is required, and the issues in al-Mabsūṭ indicate this. Thus, if the judge is unaware of the appendages, Imām Muḥammad said that the appendages do not enter the judge's judgment. Similarly, if the child is in the possession of someone else other than the rightful owner and that person is dominant, and the judge ruled the slave-girl for the rightful owner, the child does not enter that judgment as a dependent of the mother. These two issues show that the child does not enter the judgment as a dependent of the mother; rather, a separate judgment for the child is necessary.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage discusses the difference between bayyinah (evidence) and iqrār (confession/admission) in establishing ownership of a slave-girl and her child. The author explains that bayyinah is a complete proof because it reveals the original state of affairs; thus, when a rightful claimant proves ownership via bayyinah, it establishes that the slave-girl was originally his, and since the child was attached to her at that time, the child also becomes his. In contrast, iqrār is an incomplete proof; it only establishes ownership for the validity of the confession, which is fulfilled by proving ownership of the slave-girl alone, not the child. Then the passage notes an intra-Hanafi ikhtilāf among the mashāyikh: some hold that when a judge rules the slave-girl to the claimant, the child automatically follows as a dependent; others hold that a separate ruling for the child is required. The author cites Imām Muḥammad's statement that if the judge is unaware of the appendages, they do not enter the ruling, and a case where the child is in someone else's possession, indicating that the child is not included automatically. The relied-upon (muftā bihī) position is that a separate judgment for the child is necessary.

Key Terms
بینہ (bayyinah): Evidence or proof that establishes a claim; here it is a complete proof because it reveals the original state of affairs.
اقرار (iqrār): Confession or admission; here it is an incomplete proof that only establishes present ownership for necessity.
حجت کاملہ (hujjah kāmilah): Complete proof that is binding on everyone, used here to describe bayyinah.
تابع (tābiʿ): Dependent; here refers to the child being considered dependent on the mother in the ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does bayyinah establish ownership of the child while iqrār does not?
A) Because bayyinah is presented to the judge and iqrār is not.
B) Because bayyinah reveals the original state of affairs, showing the child was attached at the time of origin.
C) Because iqrār is only valid for the person confessing and not for others.
D) Because the child is a separate legal entity and cannot be included in any proof.

Key Takeaway
Bayyinah is a complete proof that reveals original ownership, thus including the child; iqrār is an incomplete proof that only establishes present ownership, not extending to the child, and a separate judgment is needed for the child in the view of the later Hanafi masters.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
10 min
Liability of a Slave Who Urges His Purchase: Sale vs. Pledge
Intra-Hanafi dispute with qiyas-based proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on a slave's liability for false self-declaration in sale and pledge, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
A slave who induces his purchase is liable only if the seller is unknown; in a pledge, he is never liable.

We often assume that anyone who causes a loss by deception should compensate it. But Hanafi fiqh distinguishes based on the nature of the transaction. This lesson explores a case from Al-Hidayah: a person claims to be a slave and urges another to buy him, but turns out free. When is he liable? The answer depends on whether the transaction is a sale (exchange) or a pledge (security), and on whether the seller can be located.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
The passage presents two scenarios: (1) a slave tells a buyer to purchase him, and (2) a slave acknowledging slavery is taken as a pledge (rahn). In each, the 'slave' is later found free.
The ruling differs between sale and pledge.
2
Note the difference in ruling
In sale: the slave is liable only if the seller is unknown (absent without trace). In pledge: the slave is never liable, according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Abu Yusuf holds him liable in both.
The key condition in sale is the seller's unknown location.
3
Locate the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Yusuf argues that the slave's false statement is the cause of harm, so liability should apply regardless of contract type. The majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) restrict liability to sale because it is an exchange contract with an implied warranty of safety.
Dispute centers on whether pledge carries a warranty.
4
Analyze the proof (qiyas)
The majority uses qiyas: analogizing the slave's command to a master's permission to merchants. In the asl, the master becomes liable when his slave turns out to belong to another; the illa is causing the transaction. In the far', the slave causes the purchase, so he is liable, but only in sale because pledge lacks the exchange element.
Abu Yusuf's objection: the illa is simply making a false statement, which exists in both.
5
Apply the principle
The practical rule: a person who commands a purchase can be held liable when the seller cannot be reached, but only in exchange contracts. In non-exchange contracts (like pledge), no warranty exists, so no liability.
This protects pledgees from undue burden.

غلام پر کچھ لازم نہ ہوگا بلکہ مشتری اپنا ثمن اپنے بائع سے واپس لے گا۔ کیونکہ مشتری سے ثمن بائع ہی نے لیا تھا اسلئے بائع ہی سے واپس لیا جائے گا۔
He said: Whoever buys a slave and then finds him free, while the slave had said to the buyer, 'Buy me, for I am a slave of his.' If the seller is present or absent in a known manner, there is nothing upon the slave. If the seller is not known where he is, the buyer has recourse against the slave, and the slave has recourse against the seller. If he takes a slave as a pledge (rahn) who acknowledges slavery and then finds him free, he cannot have recourse against him in any case. And it is narrated from Abu Yusuf that he can have recourse in both cases. Because recourse is either by muʿāwaḍah (exchange) or by kafālah (suretyship), and what exists is nothing but a false statement. So it becomes like when a stranger says that, or the slave says 'Pledge me, for I am a slave'—and that is the second issue. The proof of both (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is that the buyer began the purchase relying on his command and his acknowledgment that he is a slave, since his statement regarding freedom is admissible. Therefore, due to his command to buy, the slave is made a guarantor of the price for the buyer when recourse against the seller is impossible, to prevent deception and harm. And impossibility only occurs when the seller's location is unknown. The sale is a contract of exchange, so the one who commands it can be made a guarantor for the safety of the merchandise, as is its requirement. Contrary to a pledge (rahn), because it is not an exchange but a security for the recovery of the exact right; even a pledge for the countervalue of currency or for the subject matter of salam is valid despite the prohibition of substitution, so the one who commands a pledge is not made a guarantor for safety. And contrary to a stranger, because his statement is not heeded, so deception is not realized. An analogy to our issue is the statement of the master: 'Sell to this slave of mine, for I have given him permission,' and then entitlement (by another) becomes apparent; the merchants have recourse against the master for its value. Explanation: In this passage, two issues are mentioned. The first issue is that a man says to another, 'Buy me, I am a slave.' He buys him according to his command, then it becomes clear that the one bought as a slave is actually free. If the seller is present or absent in a known manner, nothing is owed by the slave; rather, the buyer recovers his price from the seller. Because the seller took the price, so recourse is against him.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage discusses two related issues from al-Hidayah. The first is the case of a person who tells another to buy him, claiming he is a slave. The buyer does so, but then the 'slave' turns out to be free. The ruling distinguishes based on the seller's location: if the seller is present or his whereabouts are known, the buyer recoups the price from the seller, and the slave is not liable. However, if the seller is unknown, the buyer can recover the price from the slave (the false claimant), and the slave then recovers from the seller. The second issue is when a slave acknowledging slavery is taken as a pledge (rahn) and then found free: the pledgee has no recourse against the slave. There is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Imam Abu Yusuf holds that the slave is liable in both cases (purchase and pledge) because the slave made a false statement, which is akin to a false report. But the majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) distinguish: in a sale (a contract of exchange), the one who orders the purchase (the slave) can be held liable as a guarantor when recourse against the seller is impossible, because the sale inherently involves a warranty of safety. In a pledge, which is merely a security, no such warranty exists. Also, a stranger's statement is not relied upon, so no deception occurs. The author then provides an analogy: a master who tells merchants to deal with his slave (who is a mudarrib) becomes liable if the slave turns out to be owned by someone else. This shows the principle of holding the person who caused the transaction liable for losses when the direct counterparty is unavailable.

Key Terms
رجوع (rujūʿ): Recourse; the right to recover monetary loss from another party.
غلام (ghulām): Male slave; a person owned as property.
حر (ḥurr): Free person; the opposite of slave.
ضامن (ḍāmin): Guarantor; one who is liable to pay compensation.
معاوضہ (muʿāwaḍah): Exchange contract; a transaction where one thing is given for another.
وثیقہ (wathīqah): Security; a means to secure a right, e.g., pledge (rahn).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition does the slave become liable to repay the buyer in the case of a sale?
A) When the slave admitted to being a slave.
B) When the seller is absent and his whereabouts are unknown.
C) When the buyer demands it.
D) When the slave did not inform the buyer of his freedom.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a false claimant to slavery is liable only in sale when the seller is unknown; in pledge, no liability applies due to the nature of the contract.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
10 min
Ruling on a Free Person Claiming to Be a Slave: Liability in Sale vs Pledge
Commentary on two cases with an intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a free person's liability when he falsely claims to be a slave in a sale and in a pledge, and explain the majority view and Abu Yusuf's disagreement.

Core Idea
In sale, the command to buy creates liability; in pledge, it does not — the nature of the contract determines the outcome.

This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah addresses a subtle issue: a free person tells someone 'Buy me, I am a slave.' The buyer purchases him, then discovers he is free. Can the buyer recover the price from that person? The answer depends on whether the transaction was a sale or a pledge, and whether the seller/pledgor is traceable. The Hanafi school has an internal dispute between Imam Abu Yusuf and the majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani). We will unpack the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two distinct cases: a sale (bay') and a pledge (rahn). In each, a free person claims to be a slave and either sells himself (through a seller) or is given as a pledge.
Sale is a contract of exchange (mu'awadah); pledge is a security (wathiqah).
2
State the majority ruling for each
For sale: If the seller is untraceable, the buyer can recover the price from the free person. For pledge: The pledgee cannot recover anything from the free person in any circumstance.
The condition of the seller's presence or absence is crucial in sale but irrelevant in pledge.
3
Understand Abu Yusuf's objection
Abu Yusuf argues that in both cases, the free person should not be liable because recovery requires either exchange (mu'awadah) or suretyship (kafalah), neither of which is present.
He analogizes the false statement to that of a stranger, which incurs no liability.
4
Analyse the majority's response
The majority distinguishes sale from pledge: sale is an exchange contract that inherently requires the sold item to be safe. The one who commands the purchase (the free person) is therefore liable for the price when the seller cannot be reached. Pledge lacks this exchange element.
The majority uses qiyas to the case of a master authorizing his slave to trade.
5
Determine the operative ruling (mufta bihi)
The commentary adopts the majority view, stating 'We say' to indicate preference. Thus, for fatwa, the buyer can recover from the free person in sale (if seller untraceable) but not in pledge.
This is the position of Zahir al-Riwayah.

على البائع دفعا للغرور والضرر ولا تعذر الا فيما لا يعرف مكانه والبيع عقد معاوضة فامكن ان يجعل الأمر به ضامنا للسلامة كما هو موجبه بخلاف الرهن لانه ليس بمعاوضة بل هو وثيقة لاستيفاء عين حقه يجوز الرهن ببدل الصرف والمسلم فيه مع حرمة الاستبدال فلا يجعل الآمر به ضامنا للسلامة وبخلاف الاجنبى لانه لا يعبأ بقوله فلا يتحقق الغرور ونظير مسالتنا قول المولى بايعوا عبدى هذا فاني قد اذنت له ثم ظهر الاستحقاق يرجعون عليه بقيمته
ترجمہ اور اگر کسی نے ایک غلام خریدا پھر ثابت ہوا کہ وہ آزاد ہے حالانکہ اس نے مشتری سے کہا تھا کہ تو مجھے خرید لے کہ میں اس بائع کا غلام ہوں۔ پس اگر بائع موجود ہو یا ایسے طور پر غائب ہو کہ اس کا پتہ معلوم ہو تو غلام پر کچھ لازم نہ ہوگا اور اگر بائع ایسے طور پر غائب ہو کہ اس کا پتہ نہیں معلوم کہ کہاں ہے تو مشتر کی غلام سے رجوع کرے گا اور غلام بائع سے رجوع کرے گا۔ اور اگر ایسے غلام کو رہن رکھا جو اپنے غلام ہونے کا اقرار کرتا ہے پھر مرتہن نے اس کو آزاد پایا تو مرتبہن کسی حال میں غلام سے کچھ نہیں لے سکتا اور ابو یوسف سے روایت ہے کہ غلام سے دونوں صورتوں میں واپس نہیں لے سکتا ہے۔ کیونکہ غلام سے واپس لینا معاوضہ کی وجہ سے ہوتا ہے یا کفالہ کی وجہ سے ہوتا ہے اور یہاں فقط جھوٹی خبر موجود ہے تو ایسا ہو گیا۔ جیسے کسی اجنبی نے یہ بات کہی یا غلام نے کہا کہ مجھ کو رہن رکھ لے میں غلام ہوں اور یہی دوسرا مسئلہ ہے۔
طرفین کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ مشتری نے اس کے کہنے اور اقرار کرنے پر کہ میں غلام ہوں اعتماد کر کے خریدنا شروع کیا۔ کیونکہ آزادی کے بارے میں اسی کا قول معتبر ہے۔ پس غلام کو خریدنے کا حکم دینے کی وجہ سے مشتری کے شمن کا ضامن قرار دیا جائے گا جبکہ مشتری کا بائع سے رجوع کرنا متعذر ہوتا کہ مشتری سے ضرر اور دھو کہ دور ہو اور کوئی تعذر نہیں مگر اس صورت میں کہ بائع کا ٹھکانہ معلوم نہ ہو اور بیع عقد معاوضہ ہے اسلئے اس کا حکم دینے والے کو سلامتی مبیع کا ضامن قرار دیا جائے گا جیسا کہ یہ اس کا موجب ہے برخلاف رہن کے اسلئے کہ وہ عقد معاوضہ نہیں ہے۔ بلکہ وہ عین حق وصول کرنے کا وثیقہ ہے۔ حتی کہ بدل صرف کے عوض اور مسلم فیہ کے عوض رہن جائز ہے۔ حالانکہ استبدال حرام ہے پس رہن کا حکم دینے والے کو سلامتی کا ضامن قرار نہیں دیا جا سکتا اور برخلاف اجنبی کے کیونکہ اسکے قول پر اعتبار نہیں کیا جاتا۔ اسلئے دھوکہ متحقق نہیں ہوگا۔ اور ہمارے اس مسئلہ کی نظیر یہ مسئلہ ہے کہ مولیٰ نے ( بازاری تاجروں سے ) کہا کہ تم میرے اس غلام سے خرید وفروخت کرو۔ میں نے اس کو تجارت کی اجازت دے دی۔ پھر اس غلام پر دوسرے کا استحقاق ظاہر ہوا تو تاجر لوگ مولی سے اس کی قیمت کے ( بقدر ) اینا مال واپس لیں گے۔
تشریح اس عبارت میں دو مسئلے مذکور ہیں۔
پہلا مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ ایک آدمی نے دوسرے آدمی سے کہا کہ تو مجھ کو خرید لے میں غلام ہوں ۔ اس نے اسکے حکم کے مطابق اس کو خرید لیا پھر ثابت ہوا کہ جس کو غلام سمجھ کر خریدا تھاوہ غلام نہیں ہے بلکہ آزاد ہے۔ پس اگر بائع حاضر ہو یا ایسے طور پر غائب ہو کہ اس کا ٹھ کا نا معلوم ہو تو غلام پر کچھ لازم نہ ہوگا بلکہ مشتری اپنا ثمن اپنے بائع سے واپس لے گا۔ کیونکہ مشتری سے ثمن بائع ہی نے لیا تھا اسلئے بائع ہی سے واپس لیا جائے گا۔
2

كتاب البيوع
۳۳۶
اشرف الہدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد هشتم
اور اگر بائع ایسے طور پر غائب ہو کہ اسکا ٹھکانا معلوم نہ ہو کہ کہاں ہے تو مشتری اپنا ثمن اس شخص سے واپس لے گا جس کو اس نے غلام سمجھ کر خریدا تھا پھر یہ بائع سے واپس لے گا۔ دوسرا مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ ایک شخص نے دوسرے شخص کو جو اپنے غلام ہونے کا اقرار کرتا ہے اپنے پاس رہن رکھ لیا پھر مرتہ کو معلوم ہوا کہ یہ غلام نہیں بلکہ آزاد ہے تو مرتہن کو اس غلام سے کسی صورت میں بھی اپنا مال رجوع کرنے کا اختیار نہ ہوگا اہن خواہ حاضر ہو خواہ ایسے طور پر غائب ہو کہ اس کا ٹھکانا معلوم نہ ہو، کہ کہاں ہے۔ امام ابو یوسف سے روایت ہے کہ جس طرح رہن کی صورت میں مرتبہن کو غلام سے رجوع کرنے کا اختیار نہیں ہے۔ یعنی بیع اور رہن دونوں صورتوں میں غلام سے رجوع نہیں کیا جا سکتا۔ امام ابو یوسف کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ شمن واپس لینے کا اختیار عقد معاوضہ کی وجہ سے ہوتا ہے یا کفالہ کی وجہ سے ہوتا ہے حالانکہ غلام کی طرف سے نہ معاوضہ ہے اور نہ کفالت ہے۔ معاوضہ تو اس لئے نہیں کہ غلام نے مشتری سے نہ کوئی معاملہ کیا ہے اور نہ اس سے کچھ مال لیا ہے اور کفالہ اسلئے نہیں غلام بائع کی طرف سے مشتری کیلئے من کا کفیل نہیں ہوا۔ پس جب غلام کی طرف سے نہ معاوضہ ہے اور نہ کفالہ ہے بلکہ محض ایک جھوٹی خبر ہے یعنی اس کا غلام ہونے کا اقرار کرنا ایک جھوٹی خبر ہے تو غلام شمن کا ضامن بھی نہ ہوگا اور جب ضامن نہیں ہوا تو اس سے واپس لینے کا اختیار بھی نہ ہوگا اور یہ ایسا ہو گیا جیسے کسی اجنبی نے کہا کہ یہ غلام ہے اس کو خرید نے ۔ مشتری نے اس اجنبی کے کہنے کی وجہ سے اس کو خرید لیا۔ پھر معلوم ہوا کہ وہ غلام نہیں ہے بلکہ آزاد ہے تو اجنبی پر کچھ لازم نہ ہو گا۔ پس اسی طرح اگر خود غلام نے کہا کہ میں غلام ہوں مجھ کو خرید لے۔ پھر معلوم ہوا کہ وہ آزاد ہے تو مشتری کو غلام سے ثمن واپس لینے کا اختیار نہ ہو گا۔ حضرت امام ابو یوسف بیچ کی صورت کو رہن کی صورت پر قیاس کرتے ہیں یعنی جس طرح رہن کی صورت میں مرتہن کو غلام سے رجوع کرنے کا اختیار نہیں ہوتا اسی طرح بیج کی صورت میں بھی مشتری کو غلام سے ثمن واپس لینے کا اختیار نہ ہوگا۔ طرفین کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ مشتری نے اس کے کہنے اور اقرار کرنے پر کہ میں غلام ہوں اعتماد کر کے خریدنے کا ارادہ کیا تھا کیونکہ آزاد ہونے میں اس کا قول معتبر تھا۔ پس چونکہ غلام نے مشتری کو خریدنے کا حکم دیا ہے اسلئے اس صورت میں جبکہ بائع سے ثمن واپس لینا متعذر ہو تو بقدر امکان مشتری سے ضرر اور دھو کہ دور کرنے کیلئے غلام کو مشری کے ثمن کا ضامن قرار دیا جائے گا اور بائع سے ثمن واپس لینا اس صورت میں متعد ر ہے جبک بائع کا ٹھکانا معلوم نہ ہو۔ پس ثابت ہوا کہ جب بائع کا ٹھ کا نا معلوم نہ ہو کہ کہاں ہے تو مشتری اس غلام سے لے جس نے غلام بن کر دھوکہ دیا ہے کیونکہ دھوکا دینے کی وجہ سے یہ غلام مشتری کے ثمن کا ضامن ہے۔ والبيع عقد معاوضة .. الخ سے ابو Ashraf al-Hidayah, Urdu commentary on Hidayah, Volume 8, page 335. Kitab al-Buyu'. "until" ... "upon the seller to remove deception and harm, and it is not impossible except when his location is unknown. The sale is a contract of exchange, so it is possible to make the one who commanded it liable for safety, as is its requirement, unlike the pledge because it is not an exchange but a security for recovery of his exact right. The pledge is valid for the substitute of currency and for the commodity sold despite the prohibition of substitution, so the one who commanded it is not made liable for safety. And unlike the stranger because his statement is not regarded, so deception is not realized. And the analogy to our issue is the statement of the master: 'Sell to this slave of mine, for I have permitted him,' then a claim appears; they recover its value from him." Translation: And if someone bought a slave, then it was proven that he was free, although he had said to the buyer, 'Buy me, for I am the slave of this seller.' So if the seller is present or absent in a way that his whereabouts are known, nothing is incumbent upon the slave. And if the seller is absent in a way that his whereabouts are unknown, then the buyer will recourse to the slave, and the slave will recourse to the seller. And if such a slave who acknowledges being a slave is pledged, then the pledgee finds him to be free, the pledgee cannot take anything from the slave in any case. And a narration from Abu Yusuf states that he cannot take back from the slave in both cases. Because taking back from the slave is due to exchange or due to suretyship, and here there is only a false report, so it is like when a stranger said it, or the slave said, 'Pledge me, I am a slave,' and this is the second issue. The argument of both sides: The buyer relied on his statement and acknowledgment that 'I am a slave' and began to buy, because his statement regarding freedom is authoritative. So due to his commanding the purchase, he will be made liable for the buyer's price, provided recourse to the seller is impossible, to remove harm and deception from the buyer. And impossibility only occurs when the seller's location is unknown. And sale is a contract of exchange, so the one who commands it is made liable for the safety of the sold item, as is its requirement. Unlike the pledge, because it is not a contract of exchange but a security for the recovery of his exact right. Indeed, a pledge is valid for the substitute of currency and the commodity sold despite the prohibition of substitution, so the one who commands the pledge cannot be made liable for safety. And unlike the stranger, because his statement is not relied upon, so deception is not realized. And an analogy to our issue is the issue where the master said to merchants, 'Buy and sell with this slave of mine; I have permitted him to trade.' Then a claim appears on the slave, so the merchants will recover from the master the amount of their capital. Explanation: In this text, two issues are mentioned. The first issue: A man said to another, 'Buy me, I am a slave.' He bought him according to his command. Then it was proven that the one he bought thinking he was a slave was not a slave but free. So if the seller is present or absent in a way that his location is known, nothing is incumbent on the slave; rather, the buyer will take back his price from the seller. Because the seller took the price from the buyer, so it will be taken back from him. And if the seller is absent in a way that his whereabouts are unknown, then the buyer will take his price from the person he bought thinking he was a slave, then this person will take it back from the seller. The second issue: A man pledged with himself a person who acknowledges being a slave. Then the pledgee found out that he is not a slave but free. So the pledgee has no right to recourse to the slave in any case, whether he is present or absent in a way that his location is unknown. Imam Abu Yusuf narrates that just as in the case of pledge the pledgee has no right to recourse to the slave, i.e., in both sale and pledge, recourse cannot be taken from the slave. Imam Abu Yusuf's argument: The right to take back the price is due to exchange or due to suretyship. Yet from the slave's side there is neither exchange nor suretyship. Exchange is not because the slave did not transact with the buyer nor took any wealth from him. Suretyship is not because the slave did not become surety for the price from the seller's side for the buyer. So when there is neither exchange nor suretyship from the slave's side, but merely a false report (i.e., his acknowledgment of being a slave is a false report), then the slave will not be liable for the price, and when he is not liable, there is no right to take back from him. And this is like where a stranger said, 'This is a slave, buy him.' The buyer bought him due to the stranger's statement, then it was found that he was not a slave but free; then nothing is incumbent on the stranger. So similarly, if the slave himself said, 'I am a slave, buy me,' then it was found that he is free, the buyer has no right to take back the price from the slave. Imam Abu Yusuf analogizes the case of sale to the case of pledge, meaning just as in pledge the pledgee has no right to recourse to the slave, similarly in sale the buyer has no right to take back the price from the slave. The argument of the two sides (the majority): The buyer relied on his statement and acknowledgment that 'I am a slave' to intend buying, because his statement regarding freedom was authoritative. So since the slave commanded the buyer to buy, in this case when recourse to the seller is impossible, to remove harm and deception from the buyer as much as possible, the slave will be made liable for the buyer's price. And recourse to the seller is impossible when the seller's location is unknown. So it is established that when the seller's location is unknown, the buyer takes from this slave who deceived him by pretending to be a slave, because due to deception this slave is liable for the buyer's price. From "And the sale is a contract of exchange..." to the end is a response to Abu Yusuf's argument. The gist of the response: Imam Abu Yusuf said there is no difference between sale and pledge. So just as in pledge the pledgee has no right to take back wealth from the slave, similarly in sale the buyer has no right to take back the price from the slave. We say that there is a great difference between sale and pledge. Indeed, sale is an exchange, and the requirement of a contract of exchange is that the sold item be sound and safe for the buyer. So the person who commands the buyer to buy, like this person who pretended to be a slave, can be made liable for the safety of the sold item. That is, the buyer is entitled to the sold item with a certain quality; this person, due to commanding the purchase, is liable for its safety. But because this person turned out to be free, he is liable for the price. And when he is liable for the price, then in the case of impossibility of recourse to the seller, the buyer will take his price from him. As for the pledge, it is not a contract of exchange but a means of trust and confidence.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a section from al-Hidayah dealing with two related legal issues concerning a free person who claims to be a slave, and the rulings on sale (bay') and pledge (rahn) involving such a person. It also contains an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Yusuf and the majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani). 

The first issue: A person tells another, 'Buy me, I am a slave.' The buyer purchases him, but later it is discovered that he is actually free. If the original seller (the one who sold the slave to the buyer) is present or can be located, the buyer recovers his payment from the seller, because the seller took the price. The slave (the free person) incurs no liability. However, if the seller is absent and his whereabouts are unknown, the buyer can recover the price from the free person who claimed to be a slave, and that person can then recover from the seller. The reason is that the free person's statement and command to buy created a reliance, and to prevent harm and deception, he is made liable for the price when recourse to the seller is impossible.

The second issue: If such a person (who acknowledges being a slave) is given as a pledge (rahn), and the pledgee (murtahin) later discovers he is free, the pledgee cannot recover anything from that person in any circumstance, regardless of whether the pledgor is traceable or not. This is because a pledge is not a contract of exchange but a mere security. The one who commands the pledge is not liable for the safety of the pledged item.

Imam Abu Yusuf holds that in both cases—sale and pledge—the buyer or pledgee cannot recover from the free person. His reasoning is that recovery is only justified through exchange (mu'awadah) or suretyship (kafalah). Since the free person neither received any consideration nor became a surety, his false statement alone does not create liability. He analogizes the sale case to the pledge case, arguing that a false statement by a stranger also incurs no liability, so the same applies when the free person himself speaks.

The majority (the two sides, i.e., Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) respond by distinguishing between sale and pledge. Sale is a contract of exchange, which inherently requires the sold item to be delivered safely. The one who commands the sale (the free person who claimed to be a slave) has effectively ensured the safety of the item; when he turns out to be free, he is liable for the price as an indemnity. In contrast, a pledge is not an exchange but a trust, so no such liability attaches. The commentary affirms the majority view as the operative position.

Key Terms
بیع (bay'): A contract of sale, considered a contract of exchange (mu'awadah).
رهن (rahn): A contract of pledge, where a property is given as security for a debt, not a contract of exchange.
ضمان (daman): Liability or guarantee; here, the liability of the free person to pay the price due to his false claim and command to buy.
غرور (ghurur): Deception; the false impression created by the free person that he is a slave, leading the buyer to purchase.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), in which scenario can a buyer recover the price from a free person who falsely claimed to be a slave?
A) In both a sale and a pledge, if the seller/pledgor is untraceable.
B) Only in a sale, and only when the seller is untraceable.
C) Only in a pledge, regardless of the pledgor's presence.
D) In neither case, as the free person's statement is mere speech.

Key Takeaway
In sale, the command to buy creates a liability for the safety of the sold item; in pledge, it does not, because pledge is not an exchange contract.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
12 min
Liability of a False Slave in Sale vs Pawn: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on false slave liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the liability of a person who falsely claims to be a slave in sale and pawn, and explain why the distinction hinges on whether the contract is a contract of exchange (mu'awada).

Core Idea
In sale, the false slave guarantees the price; in pawn, he does not — because sale is an exchange contract, pawn is not.

Imagine you buy a person who claims to be a slave, and later discover he is free. Can you recover your money from him if the seller disappears? Now imagine you take the same person as a pawn for a loan. Can you recover the pawned value from him? Imam Abu Yusuf argued the two cases are identical, but the majority of Hanafi jurists disagree. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this intra-madhhab dispute, focusing on the nature of the contract — exchange versus trust — and how it determines liability for deception.

Guided Path
1
State the two scenarios
Identify the two factual situations: (1) a person buys a false slave, seller untraceable; (2) a person pawns a false slave. Note the ruling in each according to the author (the mufta bihi position).
In sale: buyer recovers price from false slave. In pawn: pawner cannot recover from false slave.
2
State Abu Yusuf's position
Imam Abu Yusuf holds that in both sale and pawn, the false slave is not liable. Understand his reasoning: the right to recourse arises only from exchange or suretyship; the false slave engaged in neither.
Abu Yusuf analogizes sale to pawn and also to a stranger's false statement.
3
Understand Abu Yusuf's qiyas
Abu Yusuf uses qiyas: the pawn case is the asl (no liability), and the sale case is the far. The illa he implicitly uses is 'no exchange or suretyship involvement from the false slave'. Apply this analogy and see the result.
Qiyas leads to the same ruling for both.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response distinguishing mu'awada
The author refutes the qiyas by showing that sale (mu'awada) and pawn (non-mu'awada) have a fundamental difference. Sale requires the sold item to be sound; the person who commands the purchase guarantees that soundness. Since the false slave commanded the purchase, he guarantees the price (as a substitute for soundness). Pawn lacks this feature.
The key distinction: mu'awada creates a guarantee of soundness; non-mu'awada does not.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
Determine the practical consequence: In sale, if the seller is untraceable, the buyer can get his money from the false slave. In pawn, even if the pawner is untraceable, the pawner has no claim against the false slave. Abu Yusuf would deny recovery in both cases.
The tarjih (preferred view) is the author's, based on removing deception in exchange contracts.
