مسلم فیہ کے عوض رہن رکھنا جائز ہے حالانکہ قبضہ کرنے سے پہلے بدل صرف اور مسلم فیہ کے بدلے میں کوئی چیز لینا حرام ہے۔ اس کی صورت یہ ہے کہ خالد نے حامد کے ہاتھ دس درہم ، دس درہم کے عوض فروخت کئے اور خالد نے دس درہم حامد کو دے دیئے۔ مگر حامد نے دس درہم خالد کو نہیں دیے بلکہ دس درہم کے بدلے خالد کے پاس کوئی چیز رہن رکھ دی۔ پھر خالد مرتہن کے پاس سے وہ شیء مرہونہ ہلاک ہوگئی تو یہ کہا جائے گا کہ خالد مرتہن نے اپنا حق وصول کر لیا ہے بشرطیکہ شیء مرہونہ دس درہم کے برابر ہو۔ اور اگر شیء مرہونہ کی قیمت دس درہم سے زیادہ ہو تو دس درہم سے زائد رقم مرتہن کے پاس امانت ہوگی جس کا راہن کو واپس کرنا ضروری ہوگا۔ اور اگر شیء مرہونہ کی قیمت دس درہم سے کم ہوئی تو مرتہن یعنی خالد، راہن یعنی حامد سے باقی رقم لے لے گا۔ مسلم فیہ کی صورت یہ ہے کہ خالد نے حامد کو بیع سلم کے طور پر ایک سو درہم دیئے اور دو من گندم مسلم فیہ مقرر کئے گئے۔ پھر حامد یعنی مسلم الیہ نے خالد یعنی رب السلم کے پاس مسلم فیہ کے عوض کوئی چیز رہن رکھ دی پھر مرتہن یعنی خالد کے قبضہ میں شیء مرہونہ ہلاک ہو گئی۔ تو خالد اپنا حق وصول کرنے والا شمار ہوگا۔ بشرطیکہ شیء مرہونہ مسلم فیہ کی مالیت کے برابر ہو جیسا کہ بدل صرف میں معلوم ہوا ہے۔ بہر حال جب بدل صرف کے عوض اور مسلم فیہ کے عوض رہن رکھنا جائز نہ ہوتا اور جب رہن عقد معاوضہ نہیں ہے تو رہن کا حکم دینے والا یعنی یہ شخص جو غلام بنا تھا شیء مرہونہ کی سلامتی کا ضامن نہیں ہوتا۔ اسی وجہ سے فقہاء نے کہا ہے کہ اگر کسی نے دوسرے آدمی سے پرامن راستہ کے بارے میں دریافت کیا تو اس نے کہا کہ فلان راستہ پرامن ہے اس سے سفر کر وہ اس کے متعین کردہ راستہ سے سفر شروع کیا پس راستہ میں اسکو چوروں نے لوٹ لیا تو یہ راستہ بتلانے والا اس کے مال کا ضامن نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ راستہ بتلانے والے کی طرف سے دھوکہ ایسی چیز میں پایا گیا ہے جو عقد معاوضہ نہیں ہے۔ اسی طرح اگر کسی نے کسی سے کہا کہ یہ کھانا کھا لو اس میں زہر ملا ہوا نہیں ہے۔ پس جونہی اس نے کھایا، مر گیا، تحقیق کرنے پر پتہ چلا کہ یہ کھانا زہر آلود تھا۔ پس چونکہ یہ دھوکہ بھی عقد معاوضہ میں نہیں ہے۔ اس لئے کھانے کا حکم کرنے والا ضامن نہ ہو گا۔ بہر حال ثابت ہو گیا کہ عقد غیر معاوضہ میں دھوکہ دینے والا شیء کی سلامتی کا ضامن نہیں ہوتا اور رہن بھی چونکہ غیر معاوضہ ہے اس لئے رہن رکھنے کا حکم دینے والا یعنی یہ شخص جس نے غلام ہونے کا اقرار کیا تھا شیء مرہونہ کی سلامتی کا ضامن نہ ہوگا اور جب یہ غلام سلامتی کا ضامن نہیں ہوا تو اس کی آزادی ثابت ہونے کے بعد اس سے مرتہن کو رجوع کرنے کا اختیار بھی نہ ہوگا۔ بخلاف الاجنبی سے ابو یوسف کے قیاس کا جواب ہے جواب کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ جب اجنبی آدمی نے کسی کے بارے میں یہ کہا کہ یہ غلام ہے اس کو خرید لو تو اس کے قول پر اعتماد نہ ہو گا کیونکہ خواہ مخواہ راستہ چلتے کی بات پر اعتماد نہیں کیا جاتا اور جب اجنبی کی بات پر اعتماد نہیں ہوا تو اس کی طرف سے دھوکہ بھی متحقق نہ ہوگا اور جب دھوکہ متحقق نہیں ہوا تو غلام بیع کی آزادی ثابت ہونے کے بعد مشتری کے لئے اس پر ضمان بھی واجب نہیں ہوگا۔ کیونکہ پہلے گزر چکا ہے کہ غلام مبیع کو مشتری کے ثمن کا ضامن مشتری سے دھوکہ دور کرنے کے لئے بنایا ہے۔ مگر جب اجنبی کی طرف سے دھوکہ ہی نہیں پایا گیا تو اس کو دور کرنے کے لئے ثمن کے ضامن ہونے کا کیا سوال پیدا ہوتا ہے۔ اس اجنبی آدمی مشتری کے ثمن کا ضامن نہیں ہو گا۔ صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ متن کے مسئلہ کی نظیر یہ مسئلہ ہے کہ مولیٰ نے بازار کے تاجروں سے کہا کہ تم لوگ میرے اس غلام سے خرید و فروخت کرو۔ کیونکہ میں نے اس کو تجارت کی اجازت دے دی ہے۔ پھر معلوم ہوا کہ وہ غلام نہیں ہے بلکہ آزاد ہے درآنحالیکہ لوگوں کا اس کے ذمہ قرضہ ہے تو قرض خواہ تاجر اپنا قرض مولیٰ سے لیں گے۔ کیونکہ اس نے ان کو دھوکا دیا ہے۔ پس دھوکہ دینے کی وجہ سے مولیٰ قرض
And if the seller is absent in such a way that his whereabouts are unknown, then the buyer will take back his price from the person whom he bought thinking he was a slave; then this person will take it back from the seller. The second issue is: A person pawned another person who admits to being his slave. Then the pawner (murtahin) learns that this person is not a slave but free. In that case, the pawner has no right to reclaim his property from that (former) slave in any way, whether he is present or absent in such a way that his whereabouts are unknown. It is narrated from Imam Abu Yusuf that just as in the case of pawn the pawner has no right to recourse against the slave, similarly in both sale and pawn, recourse cannot be taken against the slave. Imam Abu Yusuf's proof is that the right to reclaim the price arises either from a contract of exchange (mu'awada) or from suretyship (kafala), whereas on the part of the slave there is neither exchange nor suretyship. As for exchange, because the slave conducted no transaction with the buyer nor took any money from him. As for suretyship, because the slave did not become surety for the price on behalf of the seller for the buyer. So since there is neither exchange nor suretyship on the part of the slave, but rather a mere false report (i.e., his admission of being a slave is a false report), the slave will not be a guarantor (dhamin) of the price. And when he is not a guarantor, he will have no right to reclaim from him. This is like when a stranger said, 'He is a slave, buy him,' and the buyer bought him on the stranger's saying, then it turns out he is not a slave but free; nothing is owed by the stranger. Similarly, if the slave himself said, 'I am a slave, buy me,' then it turns out he is free, the buyer has no right to reclaim the price from the slave. Imam Abu Yusuf analogizes the sale case to the pawn case: just as in pawn the pawner has no right of recourse against the slave, likewise in sale the buyer has no right to reclaim the price from the slave. The proof of the two sides (the author's) is that the buyer relied on his saying and admission 'I am a slave' to intend the purchase, because in matters of freedom his word is credible (so his admission of slavery is accepted as true). So since the slave commanded the buyer to buy, in this case, when reclaiming the price from the seller is impossible, to the extent possible to remove harm and deception from the buyer, the slave will be deemed a guarantor of the buyer's price. And reclaiming the price from the seller is impossible when the seller's whereabouts are unknown. So it is established that when the seller's whereabouts are unknown, the buyer takes from the slave who deceived by pretending to be a slave, because due to the deception, this slave is a guarantor of the buyer's price. (The phrase) 'And the sale is a contract of exchange...' etc. is the response to Abu Yusuf's proof. The gist of the response is that Imam Abu Yusuf said there is no difference between sale and pawn. So just as in pawn the pawner has no right to reclaim the property from the slave, similarly in sale the buyer has no right to reclaim the price from the slave. We say there is a great difference between sale and pawn. Indeed, sale is an exchange contract, and the requirement of an exchange contract is that the sold item (mabi') be sound and intact for the buyer. So the person who commands the buyer to buy, like this person who pretended to be a slave, can be made a guarantor of the soundness of the sold item. That is, the buyer is entitled to the sold item with a certain attribute; this person, due to commanding the purchase, becomes a guarantor of its soundness. But since this person turned out to be free, he becomes a guarantor of the price. And when he is a guarantor of the price, then in the case where recourse against the seller is impossible, the buyer will take back his price from him. As for pawn, it is not an exchange contract but rather a means of trust and confidence so that the pawner may obtain his exact right (i.e., the property that the pawner gave as a loan to the rahin). This is the reason why it is permissible to pawn in exchange for the countervalue in a currency exchange (badal al-sarf) and in exchange for the musallam fih (the commodity in a salam sale), even though taking something in exchange for badal al-sarf or musallam fih before possession is prohibited. The example: Khalid sold Hamid ten dirhams in exchange for ten dirhams, and Khalid gave ten dirhams to Hamid, but Hamid did not give ten dirhams to Khalid; instead he pawned something with Khalid in exchange for the ten dirhams. Then the pawned item (al-marhun) perished in Khalid's possession. It will be said that Khalid the pawner has received his right, provided the pawned item equals ten dirhams. If it exceeds ten dirhams, the excess is a trust (amana) with the pawner that must be returned to the rahin. If it is less, the pawner (Khalid) takes the balance from the rahin (Hamid). The example of musallam fih: Khalid gave Hamid one hundred dirhams as a salam sale, and two mans of wheat were appointed as the musallam fih. Then Hamid (musallam ilayh) pawned something with Khalid (rabb al-salam) in exchange for the musallam fih. Then the pawned item perished in the possession of Khalid the pawner. Then Khalid will be considered to have received his right, provided the pawned item equals the value of the musallam fih, as known in the case of badal al-sarf. In any case, since pawning in exchange for badal al-sarf and musallam fih is permissible, and since pawn is not an exchange contract, the one who commands the pawn (i.e., this person who pretended to be a slave) is not a guarantor of the soundness of the pawned item. For this reason, the jurists said: If someone asked another about a safe road, and he said 'Such-and-such road is safe, travel by it,' and he started traveling on that designated road, then thieves robbed him on the road, the one who directed him is not a guarantor of his property, because the deception on the part of the director occurred in a matter that is not an exchange contract. Similarly, if someone told another, 'Eat this food, it is not poisoned,' and he ate it and died, and upon investigation it was found that the food was poisoned, then because this deception also did not occur in an exchange contract, the one who commanded the eating is not a guarantor. In any case, it is established that in a non-exchange contract, the deceiver is not a guarantor of the soundness of the thing. And since pawn is also a non-exchange contract, the one who commands the pawn (i.e., this person who admitted to being a slave) is not a guarantor of the soundness of the pawned item. And when this slave is not a guarantor of soundness, after his freedom is established, the pawner has no right of recourse against him. (The response) to Abu Yusuf's analogy of the stranger: The gist is that when a stranger says about someone, 'He is a slave, buy him,' his statement is not relied upon because one does not rely on the casual words of a passerby. And when the stranger's statement is not relied upon, deception on his part is also not realized. And when deception is not realized, after the buyer's freedom is established, guarantee (daman) is not obligatory upon him. Because it has passed earlier that the slave of the sold item is made a guarantor of the buyer's price to remove deception from the buyer. But when no deception from the stranger is found, what question arises of his being a guarantor of the price to remove it? This stranger will not be a guarantor of the buyer's price. The author of al-Hidayah says: The analogy of the text's issue is this issue: A master (mawla) told the merchants in the market, 'You people transact with my slave, for I have given him permission to trade.' Then it turned out that he was not a slave but free, while the people have a debt owed by him. Then the creditor merchants will take their debt from the master, because he deceived them. So due to the deception, the master (is liable for) the debt.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Yusuf and the author (the commentator of al-Hidayah) regarding the liability of a person who falsely claims to be a slave in two different contracts: sale and pawn. The author opens by stating the main ruling: if a buyer purchases a person believing him to be a slave, but the seller is untraceable, the buyer may recover the price from the false slave (who then recovers from the seller). In contrast, in a pawn, the pawner (murtahin) cannot recover from the false slave regardless of the pawner's presence or absence. Imam Abu Yusuf holds that both sale and pawn are identical: in neither case can recourse be taken against the false slave. His reasoning is that the right to reclaim the price arises only from a contract of exchange (mu'awada) or suretyship (kafala), and the false slave engaged in neither. The false slave's statement is merely a false report, akin to a stranger's false statement; neither creates liability. The author refutes this by distinguishing sale from pawn. Sale is a contract of exchange (mu'awada), which obligates the seller to deliver the sold item (mabi') in sound condition. The person who commands the purchase (i.e., the false slave) becomes a guarantor of that soundness. Since the false slave turned out to be free, he guarantees the price instead. In contrast, pawn is not an exchange contract; it is a trust-based arrangement to secure a debt. Therefore, the one who commands the pawn (the false slave) does not guarantee the pawned item's soundness. The author supports this distinction with examples: pawning in exchange for the countervalue in currency exchange (sarf) and the commodity in salam is valid, proving that pawn is not a pure exchange. Further, the author provides two illustrations of deception in non-exchange contexts (giving road directions and inviting to eat poisoned food) where the deceiver is not liable. The author also addresses Abu Yusuf's analogy with a stranger: the stranger's statement is not relied upon, so no deception exists, hence no liability. The passage concludes with a precedent from the text of al-Hidayah: if a master permits his slave to trade and the slave turns out to be free, the master is liable for debts incurred by the freedman because the master deceived the traders. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is the author's view: in sale, the false slave guarantees the price when the seller is untraceable; in pawn, no recourse against the false slave.

Key Terms
عقد معاوضہ (aqd mu'awada): A contract of exchange where each party gives and receives something (e.g., sale).
کفالہ (kafalah): Suretyship; a person becomes a guarantor for another's obligation.
ضمان (daman): Liability; being responsible for a debt or loss.
رہن (rahn): Pawn; a contract where property is given as security for a debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi Hanafi position, what is the ruling if a person pawns a 'slave' (who is actually free) with a pawner, and the pawner is untraceable?
A) The pawner can recover the pawned property from the false slave.
B) The pawner cannot recover the pawned property from the false slave.
C) The false slave must pay the value of the pawned property to the pawner.
D) The pawner must pursue the false slave only if the pawner was present at the time of pawn.

Key Takeaway
The key to the false slave's liability lies in whether the contract is a mu'awada: in sale, he guarantees the price; in pawn, he does not.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Guarantee of the Seller When a Slave Proves Free After Purchase
Intra-Hanafi dispute on liability for deception in sale of a slave falsely represented as such.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability for deception when a sold slave proves free, and distinguish between cases of authoritative inducement versus casual statements.

Core Idea
Deception triggers liability only when the statement is authoritative enough to induce reliance — not for casual remarks.

This passage addresses a critical issue in sales law: when a sold slave is later proven to be free, who bears the loss? The Hanafi school distinguishes between authoritative inducement (e.g., a seller or master) and casual statements (e.g., a stranger). The discussion reveals an intra-madhhab objection to Imam Abu Hanifa's view on claims and contradiction, which is resolved through nuanced analysis of original freedom versus manumission. Understanding this ruling sharpens your ability to apply the principle of taghrir (deception) in transactions.

Guided Path
1
State the general principle
Identify the rule: In a non-compensatory contract, the deceiver is not liable for the safety of the thing. Here, pawn (rahn) is non-compensatory, so the one who ordered the pawn (the person admitting to be a slave) is not liable. However, if the seller actively induces the sale by representing the slave as his, liability arises.
The distinction between compensatory (uqd al-mu'awadah) and non-compensatory contracts is key.
2
Examine the analogy to the master
The author cites a precedent: a master who tells merchants to trade with his slave, then the slave proves free. The master is liable up to the slave's value because he deceived them. This is the core analogy for the seller's liability.
The analogy relies on the common element of authoritative inducement (amr or tasdit).
3
Analyze the objection to Abu Hanifa
The objection: The slave first says 'I am a slave' (to induce purchase), then claims freedom. This is a contradiction (tanqud), which invalidates the claim of freedom under Abu Hanifa's rule that a claim (dawa) is required. Without a valid claim, freedom is not proven, so the buyer cannot recover.
This objection challenges the very fact scenario of the ruling.
4
Evaluate the two responses
First response: If the slave's freedom is original (hurriyah asliyyah), Abu Hanifa does not require a claim because it involves a right of Allah (prohibition of the mother's private part). Second response: If the freedom is via manumission (itq), contradiction does not prevent because the master's action is independent, analogous to a woman proving divorces before khul' or a mukatab proving manumission before kitabah.
These responses preserve the ruling despite the logical objection.

In any case, it has been established that in a non-compensatory contract, the deceiver is not the guarantor of the safety of the thing. And since pawn (rahn) is non-compensatory, therefore the one who ordered the pawn to be given—meaning the person who admitted to being a slave—is not the guarantor of the safety of the pawned item. And when this slave is not the guarantor of safety, after his freedom is proven, the mortgagee (murtahin) will have no right to recourse against him. As for the stranger, this is a response to Abu Yusuf's analogy. The gist of the response is that when a stranger says about someone, 'He is a slave, buy him,' his statement will not be relied upon because random talk on the road is not trusted. And when the stranger's statement is not trusted, then deception from his side is not realized. And when deception is not realized, then after the slave's freedom is proven, the buyer is not obligated to pay (daman) to him. Because it has previously passed that the sold slave is made a guarantor of the price for the buyer to remove deception from the buyer. But when no deception is found from the stranger, what question arises of his being a guarantor of the price to remove it? This stranger will not be the guarantor of the price for the buyer. The author of Hidayah says: The precedent for the issue of the text is this issue: That a master said to the merchants in the market, 'You people buy and sell with this slave of mine, because I have given him permission to trade.' Then it was discovered that he is not a slave but free, while people have debts on him. So the creditors and merchants will take their debt from the master, because he deceived them. Due to his deception, the master will be the guarantor of the creditors. But note that the master will be guarantor to the extent of the slave's value, not more. Objection to Imam's position: Then in the setting of the issue, there is an objection to Abu Hanifa's statement, because according to him, a claim is a condition for the slave's freedom, and contradiction invalidates the claim. And it is said: If the setting concerns original freedom, then a claim is not a condition for it according to him, because it entails the prohibition of the mother's private part. And it is said: It is a condition, but contradiction is not a preventer due to the concealment of the drop's attachment. And if the setting concerns manumission, then contradiction does not prevent because of the master's independence in it. So it becomes like a woman who takes khul' establishes witnesses for three divorces before khul', and a slave under contract of manumission (mukatab) establishes witnesses for manumission before the contract. Translation: Then in this mentioned issue, there is a kind of objection to Imam Abu Hanifa's statement. Because according to Imam, a claim is a condition for the slave's freedom, and contradiction invalidates the claim. And it was said: If the issue is assumed concerning original freedom, then according to Imam, a claim is not a condition for it, because this freedom entails the prohibition of the mother's private part. And some said: The claim is a condition, but contradiction is not a preventer because the attachment of the sperm is hidden. And if the mentioned issue is assumed concerning manumission, then contradiction is not a preventer because the master is independent in manumission. So this becomes like a woman who takes khul' establishes witnesses for three divorces before khul', and a mukatab establishes witnesses for manumission before the contract of kitabah. Explanation: The author of Hidayah says: There is a kind of objection to Imam Abu Hanifa's statement in the issue of the text. Before the objection, keep in mind that according to Imam Abu Hanifa, to prove the slave's freedom, making a claim of freedom is a condition, meaning the slave must first be a claimant of freedom, then his testimony will be heard. The second point is that contradiction in a claim invalidates the claim. The objection: The explanation of the objection is that the slave who said to the buyer, 'I am this seller's slave, so buy me,' and then made a claim before the buyer that 'I am free' and established witnesses on being free, then the slave first claimed to be a slave, then claimed to be free, and this is clear contradiction. And the claim of a person in which there is contradiction is not correct. And when the slave's claim of freedom is not correct, then his witnesses will not be accepted. And when the witnesses of freedom are not accepted, then the slave's freedom is not proven. And when the slave's freedom is not proven, what question arises of the buyer taking back the price? In any case, according to Imam A'zam Abu Hanifa, the issue does not take shape. The author has given two answers to this objection. The first answer is that if in the text's statement 'he is free' the freedom intended is original and birth freedom, then the consensus of the leading scholars is that according to Imam Abu Hanifa, a claim is not a condition for proving original freedom, because when a person is originally free, his mother is not owned by anyone. And when his mother is not owned by anyone, then her private part is forbidden to the person who claims that she is his slave. And the prohibition of the private part is among the rights of Allah, and for establishing the rights of Allah, a claim is not a condition; rather, without a claim...
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf Al-Hidayah discusses the liability of a seller when a slave, after being sold, proves to be free. The author establishes a general principle: in a non-compensatory contract, the deceiver is not liable for the safety of the subject matter. Since pawn (rahn) is non-compensatory, the person who instructed the pawn (the one who admitted to being a slave) is not liable if the pawned item is destroyed. Consequently, if the slave later proves free, the mortgagee cannot recover from him. The text then contrasts this with the case of a stranger who merely says someone is a slave and advises purchase. Such a statement is not reliable, so no deception is established, and the stranger is not liable for the price. The author provides a precedent from the Hidayah: a master who tells merchants to trade with his slave, but the slave turns out to be free. In that case, the master is liable to the creditors up to the slave's value due to his deception. The passage then raises an objection to Imam Abu Hanifa's position. According to Abu Hanifa, a claim (dawa) is a condition for proving a slave's freedom, and contradiction invalidates the claim. In the scenario, the slave first said he was a slave, then claimed freedom—a clear contradiction. Thus, under Abu Hanifa's view, the claim of freedom would be invalid, witnesses not accepted, and freedom unproven, so the buyer cannot recover. The author provides two answers: 1) If the freedom is original (not via manumission), Abu Hanifa does not require a claim because original freedom entails prohibition of the mother's private part, which is a right of Allah established without claim. 2) If the freedom is via manumission, contradiction does not prevent because the master's action is independent, analogous to a wife proving divorces before khul' or a mukatab proving manumission before kitabah. Thus, the objection is resolved, and the ruling stands: the person who instructed the sale is liable as a guarantor.

Key Terms
دعوی (dawa): A formal claim made by a person asserting a right, here the slave's claim of freedom.
تناقض (tanqud): Contradiction in statements, which invalidates a legal claim.
حریت اصلیہ (hurriyah asliyyah): Original freedom from birth, as opposed to freedom acquired through manumission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a stranger not liable when he tells someone to buy a person claiming 'he is a slave' and the person turns out to be free?
A) Because a stranger's statement is considered reliable.
B) Because a stranger's statement is not relied upon, so no deception is established.
C) Because the buyer should have investigated himself.
D) Because the stranger did not take any money.

Key Takeaway
The seller who induces a transaction by falsely presenting a person as a slave is liable for the price up to the slave's value due to deception.

Resolving Contradiction in a Slave's Claim of Freedom
Resolving the contradiction in a slave's claim of freedom after acknowledging slavery

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Hanafi jurists excuse contradiction (tanāquḍ) in a slave's claim of freedom in certain circumstances and apply the two resolutions to similar legal scenarios.

Core Idea
Contradiction in a claim is excused if the earlier statement was based on hidden circumstances.

In Hanafi fiqh, a claim (daʿwā) is a procedural requirement for proving a right, and contradiction generally invalidates it. However, this passage from Ashraf al-Hidāya reveals an important exception: when a slave first acknowledges slavery but later claims freedom, his contradictory statements can be reconciled due to the hidden nature of lineage or the master's independent action. This lesson unpacks the objection and two resolutions from the mashā'ikh.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, for proving a slave's freedom, a formal claim (daʿwā) is required. Additionally, any contradiction (tanāquḍ) in the claim invalidates it. Identify these two principles.
This sets up the conflict: the slave's statements are contradictory.
2
Identify the objection
The slave first says 'I am a slave' to the buyer, then later claims 'I am free' and provides witnesses. This is a clear contradiction, so under Abu Hanifa's rule, the claim of freedom should be invalid, and his witnesses rejected. Recognize why this poses a problem for his freedom being proven.
The objection is that the scenario in the text (slave claims freedom after acknowledging slavery) cannot hold under Abu Hanifa's principles.
3
Analyze the first resolution
If the freedom claimed is original freedom (ḥurriyya aṣliyya), then a claim is not required because original freedom implies the prohibition of the mother's private part, a right of Allah. Rights of Allah are proven without a claim. Therefore, no contradiction arises.
Key distinction: original freedom vs. acquired freedom.
4
Analyze the second resolution
Even if a claim is required, contradiction is excused due to hidden circumstances. For original freedom, the slave may be unaware of his lineage; for emancipation, the master's action may be unknown to the slave. The initial acknowledgment was based on ignorance, so the later claim is valid.
Analogy: khulʿ case and mukātib case show similar excused contradictions.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: Hanafis accept the slave's claim of freedom and allow the buyer to recover the price, while those who reject the claim would not. Understand which resolution is more widely applied.
The second resolution ('contradiction excused') appears to be the muftā bihī.

دیا لہذا اس میں کسی طرح کا کوئی تناقض نہیں ہے۔
Then, in the scenario of the issue, an objection arises against Abu Hanifa's statement because according to him, a claim (da'wa) is a condition for the freedom of a slave, and contradiction (tanāquḍ) invalidates the claim. It was said: if the scenario assumes original freedom (ḥurriyya aṣliyya), then a claim is not a condition according to him because it implies the prohibition of the mother's private part (farj). And it was said: he makes it a condition, but contradiction is not an impediment due to the concealment of the drop (ʿuluq). And if the scenario assumes emancipation (iʿtāq), then contradiction does not prevent due to the master's absolute authority over it. Thus, it becomes like a woman who takes khulʿ and then establishes evidence of three divorces before the khulʿ, and a mukātib who establishes evidence of emancipation before the kitāba. Translation: Then, in the scenario of this mentioned issue, there is a kind of objection to Imam Abu Hanifa's statement. Because according to Imam Sahib, a claim (da'wa) is a condition for the freedom of a slave. And contradiction invalidates the claim. And it was said that if the issue is assumed in original freedom, then according to Imam Sahib, a claim is not a condition in it, because this freedom implies the prohibition of the mother's private part. And some said that the claim is indeed a condition, but contradiction is not an impediment because the settling of the drop (nutfa) is hidden. And if the mentioned issue is assumed in emancipation (iʿtāq), then contradiction is not an impediment because the master is independent in emancipating. Thus, this becomes like a woman who takes khulʿ and then establishes witnesses of three divorces before the khulʿ, and a mukātib establishes witnesses of emancipation before the kitāba. Explanation: The author of Hidayah says that there is a kind of objection to Imam Abu Hanifa's statement in the issue of the text. Before the objection, note that according to Imam Abu Hanifa, for proving a slave's freedom, it is necessary to claim freedom first, i.e., the slave must first be a claimant of freedom, then his testimony is heard. Second point: contradiction (tanāquḍ) in a claim invalidates the claim. The objection: The explanation of the objection is that the slave who said to the buyer, 'I am the slave of this seller, so buy me,' and then claimed before the buyer, 'I am free,' and established witnesses of his freedom, then the slave first claimed to be a slave and then claimed to be free, and this is a clear contradiction. And a person whose claim contains contradiction, that claim is not valid. And when the slave's claim of freedom is not valid, his witnesses will also not be accepted. And when the witnesses of freedom are not accepted, the slave's freedom is not proven. And when the slave's freedom is not proven, then what question arises of the buyer returning the price? In any case, according to Imam Abu Hanifa, the scenario of the issue does not hold. The author has given two answers to this objection. First answer: If by 'ḥurriyya' in the text's statement 'fa-idhā huwa ḥurr' (then he is free) is meant original and birth freedom, then according to the generality of the mashā'ikh, according to Imam Abu Hanifa, a claim is not a condition for proving original freedom because when a person is originally free, his mother is not owned by anyone, and when his mother is not owned by anyone, then her private part is forbidden to the person who claims that she is his slave, and the prohibition of the private part is among the rights of Allah (ḥuqūq Allāh), and for proving rights of Allah, a claim is not a condition; rather, witnesses' testimony is accepted without a claim. The outcome is that original freedom implies the prohibition of the mother's private part, and since for the prohibition of the private part a claim is not a condition, therefore for original freedom which implies the prohibition of the private part, a claim will also not be a condition. And when a claim is not a condition for original freedom, then contradiction will not necessarily arise in the statement of the person who said 'ishtaraytanī fa-ana 'abd' (you bought me, so I am a slave). Second answer: Some mashā'ikh say that a claim is also a condition for proving original freedom, but then contradiction will occur. Because first he acknowledged being a slave, and now he claims freedom. The response to this is that here contradiction is not an impediment to the validity of the claim; rather, it is excused. Because the settling of the drop (nutfa) is a hidden matter, for example, he was brought from Dār al-Ḥarb as a child, and he was unaware of the freedom status of his parents, so he acknowledged being a slave. Later he learned that his parents were free, so he claimed his freedom. In any case, the settling of the drop and nutfa is a hidden matter, and in hidden matters, contradiction is excused. Therefore, here too, contradiction will be excused. And if by the text's statement 'fa-idhā huwa ḥurr' is meant the freedom obtained through the master's emancipation, then in this case too, contradiction will not prevent the validity of the claim. Because the master is independent in emancipating his slave. So it is possible that when the master emancipated him, some people knew, but the slave himself was unaware. Then when the master sold him, he acknowledged being a slave out of ignorance. Then when he inquired from people, he established witnesses of his freedom and claimed that he was emancipated. This becomes like a woman who took khulʿ from her husband and then later established witnesses that her husband had given her three divorces before the khulʿ; her witnesses are accepted, and there is no contradiction. Because the husband is independent in effecting divorce; it is possible that the woman was unaware of the divorce before the khulʿ and later came to know. Similarly, a slave entered into kitāba with his master and then established witnesses that his master had emancipated him before the kitāba; his witnesses are accepted. So similarly, in the issue of the text, when a person acknowledged being a slave to the buyer, he can say: 'I was unaware of the master's emancipation at that time, so I acknowledged being a slave, but when I later came to know, I claimed freedom.' Therefore, there is no contradiction whatsoever.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidāya is an expansion of a ruling in al-Hidāya regarding a slave who first acknowledges being a slave to a buyer, then claims freedom and provides witnesses. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, a claim (da'wa) is a prerequisite for proving freedom, and any contradiction (tanāquḍ) in the claim invalidates it. The objection is that the slave's initial acknowledgment and later claim form a contradiction, so his freedom cannot be proven. The author presents two resolutions: (1) If the freedom is original (ḥurriyya aṣliyya), then a claim is not required because it involves the prohibition of the mother's private part, a right of Allah, which can be proven without a claim. Thus, no contradiction arises. (2) If the freedom is through emancipation (iʿtāq), or even if a claim is required for original freedom, the contradiction is excused due to the hidden nature of lineage or the master's independent action. The slave's initial acknowledgment may be based on ignorance, and later knowledge allows a valid claim. Examples from khulʿ and kitāba illustrate that contradiction is not always fatal.

Key Terms
دعوى (daʿwā): A formal legal claim made by a person to establish a right; in this context, the slave's claim of freedom before witnesses.
تناقض (tanāquḍ): Contradiction between two statements; here, the slave's statement 'I am a slave' conflicts with his later claim 'I am free'.
حريت اصلية (ḥurriyya aṣliyya): Original freedom, i.e., born free, as opposed to being emancipated.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first resolution in the passage, why is a claim (da'wa) not required for proving original freedom?
A) Because original freedom is obvious and needs no proof.
B) Because original freedom implies the prohibition of the mother's private part, which is a right of Allah.
C) Because the buyer has no standing to contest the slave's freedom.
D) Because the slave's acknowledgment of slavery is considered a lie.

Key Takeaway
Contradiction in a claim is not always fatal; Hanafi jurists excuse it when the earlier statement was based on hidden circumstances, such as ignorance of lineage or the master's independent action.

Compromise for an Unknown Right: When the Property Turns Out to Belong to Another
Ruling on compromise for an unknown right when the house is later found to belong mostly to a third party

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a compromise is made for an unknown right in a house that later is found to belong mostly to a third party, and explain the legal reasoning (ʿilla) that prevents the possessor from recovering the payment.

Core Idea
An unknown right claim is interpreted as covering only the part actually owned — the compromise stands.

This passage from al-Hidāyah addresses a practical scenario: a person claims an unspecified right in a house, the possessor pays a sum to settle the claim, and later it turns out the house belongs to someone else except a small portion. The key question is whether the possessor can get his money back. The Hanafi ruling protects the claimant's right to allocate his claim to the portion he actually owned, making the compromise binding for that portion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their actions
There is a claimant who asserts an unspecified right (ḥaqq majhūl) in a house. The possessor of the house enters into a compromise (ṣulḥ) with him, paying 100 dirhams to settle the claim. The right is unknown — neither the nature nor the location is specified.
The ambiguity is deliberate; it allows the claimant to later interpret his claim narrowly.
2
Note the subsequent discovery
After the compromise, it is proven that the house actually belongs to a third party, except for one yard (dhirāʿ) which indeed belongs to the claimant. So the claimant had a real, but very small, right.
The claimant owned a small part of the house, not the whole.
3
Determine the legal consequence for the possessor
The possessor wants to recover the 100 dirhams he paid, arguing that the house turned out not to be the claimant's. The ruling states he cannot recover anything.
The possessor's payment is lost.
4
Understand the legal reasoning (ʿilla)
The claimant can say: 'My claim was only for this one yard.' Since the claim was unknown, it is interpreted in the way that preserves the validity of the compromise. The compromise is effectively a sale of the claimant's actual right for the sum paid.
The ambiguity works in the claimant's favor — he can allocate the claim to the part he actually owned.
5
Apply the ruling to similar cases
This ruling applies whenever a compromise is made for an unknown right and the claimant turns out to own a portion of the disputed property. The possessor cannot recover the payment for that portion.
If the claimant had no right at all, the ruling might differ (the compromise would be void).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ومن ادعى حقا في دار معناه حقا مجهولا فصالحه الذى فى يده على مائة درهم فاستحقت الدار الاذراعا منها لم يرجع بشتى لان للمدعى ان يقول دعواى في هذا الباقي. ترجمہ: اور اگر کسی نے ایک گھر میں کچھ حق کا دعویٰ یعنی حق مجہول کا دعویٰ کیا پھر قابض نے مدعی سے سو درہم پر صلح کر لی پھر اس گھر میں سے علاوہ ایک گز کے مستحق ہو گیا تو قابض مدعی سے کچھ مال واپس نہیں لے سکتا ہے۔ کیونکہ مدعی کیلئے یہ کہنے کا حق ہے کہ میرا دعویٰ اسی ایک گز میں تھا۔
He said: And whoever claims a right in a house, meaning an unknown right, and the possessor makes a compromise with him for one hundred dirhams, then the house is proven to belong to someone else except one yard, he cannot recover anything because the claimant can say, 'My claim was in this remaining part.' Translation: And if someone claims a right in a house, i.e., an unknown right, then the possessor compromises with the claimant for one hundred dirhams, then the house turns out to be owned by someone else except one yard, then the possessor cannot recover any money from the claimant, because the claimant has the right to say: 'My claim was only in this one yard.'
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage from al-Hidāyah states a ruling about a claim of an unknown right (ḥaqq majhūl) in a house. The possessor of the house enters into a compromise (ṣulḥ) with the claimant, paying him 100 dirhams to settle the claim. Later, it is discovered that the house actually belongs to a third party, except for one yard (dhirāʿ) which indeed belonged to the claimant. The question is whether the possessor can reclaim the 100 dirhams from the claimant. The ruling is that he cannot recover anything, because the claimant can legitimately argue that his original claim was specifically for that one yard. Since the compromise was for an unknown right, it can be interpreted as covering only the right that actually existed. The possessor's payment is considered compensation for the claimant's right in that yard. The 'illa (legal reasoning) is that the claim was ambiguous, and the compromise is valid for whatever right the claimant actually possessed.

Key Terms
حق مجهول (ḥaqq majhūl): An unspecified or unknown right; the claimant does not specify the exact nature or location of his claimed interest.
صلح (ṣulḥ): A compromise or settlement whereby a party pays a sum to resolve a dispute without litigation.
استحقاق (istiḥqāq): Proven entitlement or ownership; here, the house is proven to belong to a third party.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why can the possessor not recover the 100 dirhams from the claimant when only one yard of the house was actually owned by the claimant?
A) Because the compromise was invalid from the start.
B) Because the claimant can argue that his claim was only for that one yard.
C) Because the possessor failed to verify ownership before compromise.
D) Because the third-party owner is liable for the payment.

Key Takeaway
A compromise for an unknown right remains valid for the part actually owned; the payment is not recoverable.

Sulh for an Unknown Right: Possessor Cannot Recover
Explains the first scenario and its ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a sulh for an unknown right is made and a third party takes most of the property, and explain why the possessor cannot recover the payment.

Core Idea
Sulh for an unknown right: payment stands if claimant can attribute his claim to the remaining portion.

This lesson examines the first scenario in al-Hidayah regarding sulh (compromise) when a claimant asserts an unspecified right in a house. After settling for a known sum, a third party proves ownership of almost the entire house. The key question: can the possessor reclaim his payment? The Hanafi ruling provides a nuanced answer based on the flexibility of jahalah (ignorance) in matters of relinquishment (isqat).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a sulh (compromise) where the possessor pays 100 dirhams to settle a claim of an unknown right. After a third party establishes ownership of the house except one yard, the possessor cannot recover any payment. The ruling is that the sulh is valid and the payment is final.
This is a ruling with proof; the text provides a rational justification.
2
Locate the dalil
The dalil is the claimant's assertion that his claim was only in the remaining one yard. The text states: 'because the claimant can say: my claim was in this remaining (yard).' This rational inference is supported by the Hanafi principle that ignorance in what is relinquished (isqat) does not lead to dispute.
The proof is rational (istihsan) rather than a direct text.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The claimant's ability to attribute his original claim to the one yard that remains means that he had a valid right in that part. Since the sulh was for relinquishing that right, the possessor cannot reclaim the payment. The payment is considered compensation for that valid right.
This reasoning prevents contestation after the fact.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The Hanafi position permits sulh for an unknown right. Other schools may require the right to be specified to avoid gharar. However, the Hanafi view argues that jahalah in isqat does not cause dispute, so the sulh is valid.
This is a mukhtalaf fih issue between madhahib.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The ruling in al-Hidayah represents the mufta bihi position. The text uses rational evidence to support the validity of the sulh and the non-recovery. The tarjih authority is the text itself (al-Hidayah), which is based on Zahir al-Riwayah.
This is the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school.

Source Text

کسی نے گھر میں مجہول حق کا دعوی کیا مدعی نے سو درہم پر صلح کر لی پھر گھر میں سوائے ایک گز کے کوئی مستحق نکل آیا تو قابض مدعی سے کچھ واپس نہیں لے سکتا قال ومن ادعى حقا في دار معناه حقا مجهولا فصالحه الذى فى يده على مائة درهم فاستحقت الدار الاذراعا منها لم يرجع بشتى لان للمدعى ان يقول دعواى في هذا الباقي ترجمہ اور اگر کسی نے ایک گھر میں کچھ حق کا دعوئی یعنی حق مجہول کا دعوی کیا پھر قابض نے مدعی سے سو درہم پر صلح کر لی پھر اس گھر میں سے علاوہ ایک گز کے مستحق ہو گیا تو قابض مدعی سے کچھ مال واپس نہیں لے سکتا ہے۔ کیونکہ مدعی کیلئے یہ کہنے کا حق ہے کہ میرا دعوئی اسی ایک گز میں تھا۔

اشرف الہدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد ہشتم تشریح مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ ایک مکان ایک آدمی کے قبضہ میں ہے دوسرے آدمی نے اس مکان میں اپنے حق مجہول کا دعوی کیا اور قابض یعنی مدعلی علیہ نے انکار کیا پھر قابض نے مدعی سے ایک سو درہم پر صلح کی یعنی قابض نے ایک دوسو درہم مدعی کو دے کر اس کا حق مجبول لے لیا۔ پھر ایک تیسرے آدمی نے سوائے ایک گز کے اس پورے گھر پر اپنا استحقاق ثابت کر دیا تو قابض یعنی مدعی علیہ کو مدعی سے کچھ مال واپس لینے کا اختیار نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ مدعی یہ کہہ سکتا ہے کہ میرا دعومی اسی ایک گز میں تھا جس کے عوض میں نے ایک سو درہم لئے ہیں۔
اگر کل کا دعویٰ کیا اور سو درہم پر صلح کر لی پھر کسی چیز کا کوئی مستحق نکل آیا تو
اسی حساب سے رجوع کرے گا
وان ادعاها كلها فصالحه على مائة درهم فاستحق منها شيء رجع بحسابه لان التوفيق غير ممكن فوجب الرجوع ببدله عند قوات سلامة المبدل ودلت المسألة على ان الصلح عن المجهول على معلوم جائز لان الجهالة فيما يسقط لا تفضى الى المنازعة
ترجمہ اور اگر مدعی نے پورے گھر کا دعوی کیا پھر قابض نے سوار ہم پر اس سے صلح کر لی پھر گھر کا ایک حصہ مستحق ہو گیا تو قابض مدعی سے اسی کے حساب سے واپس لے گا۔ اسلئے کہ توفیق ناممکن ہے۔ پس سلامتی کمبدل کے فوت ہونے کے وقت اس کے بدل کا واپس لینا واجب ہوا۔ اور یہ مسئلہ اس بات پر دلیل ہے کہ مجہول چیز کے بارے میں مال معلوم پر صلح کرنا جائز ہے۔ اسلئے کہ جہالت اس چیز میں ساقط ہو جاتی ہے جو مفضی الی المنازعہ نہیں ہوتی ۔
تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ ایک آدمی کے قبضہ میں ایک مکان ہے دوسرے آدمی نے پورے مکان پر دعویٰ کیا پس قابض نے مدعی سے ایک سو درہم پر صلح کر لی یعنی پورے مکان کے عوض سو درہم دے دیئے۔ پھر اس مکان کے ایک حصہ پر کسی تیسرے آدمی نے اپنا استحقاق ثابت کر دیا مثلاً آدھا مکان استحقاق ثابت کر کے لے لیا تو اس صورت میں قابض یعنی مدعی علیہ، مدعی سے ایک سو درہم کا آدھا یعنی پچاس درہم واپس لے لے گا۔ کیونکہ استحقاق ثابت ہونے کے بعد معلوم ہو گیا کہ مدعی نے یہ پچاس درہم بغیر کسی حق کے لئے تھے۔ صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ یہ مسئلہ اس بات پر دلیل ہے کہ شی مجہول کے بارے میں مال معلوم پر صلح کرنا جائز ہے کیونکہ جہالت ان چیزوں میں جو ساقط ہو جاتی ہیں جھگڑا پیدا نہیں کرتی اور جو جہالت جھگڑا پیدا نہ کرے وہ عدم جواز کا سبب نہیں ہوتی اور یہاں یہی بات ہے۔ اسلئے کہ جب مدعی نے مکان میں حق مجہول کا دعوی کیا اور قابض نے اس سے ایک سو درہم پر صلح کر لی تو گویا مدعی نے ایک سو درہم کے عوض اپنا حق ساقط کر دیا ہے اور اسقاطات میں جہالت مفضی الی المنازعہ نہیں ہوتی اور جو جہالت مفضی الی المنازعہ نہ ہو وہ عدم جواز کا سب بھی نہیں ہوتی۔ اسلئے مال معلوم پر مجہول چیز کے بارے میں صلح کرنا بھی جائز ہوگا۔ دوسرے یہ مسئلہ اس بات پر دلالت کرتا ہے کہ صحت صلح کیلئے صحت دعوئی شرط نہیں ہے۔ کیونکہ مکان کے اندر حق مجہول کا دعوئی جہالت کی وجہ سے صحیح نہیں ہے۔ حالانکہ صلح صحیح ہے۔ پس معلوم ہوا کہ صحت صلح کیلئے صحت دعوئی شرط نہیں ہے۔ واللہ اعلم جمیل احمد غفرلہ
Someone claimed an unknown right in a house; the claimant made a compromise (sulh) for one hundred dirhams; then a third party became entitled to the entire house except one yard (dhira‘). The possessor cannot take anything back from the claimant. He (the author) said: And whoever claims a right in a house, meaning an unknown right, and the one in possession makes a compromise with him for one hundred dirhams, then the house is found to belong to someone else except a yard of it, he (the possessor) does not get back anything, because the claimant can say: my claim was in this remaining (yard). Translation: And if someone claims a right in a house, i.e., an unknown right, then the possessor compromises with the claimant for one hundred dirhams, then a third party establishes entitlement to the entire house except one yard, then the possessor cannot take back any wealth from the claimant, because the claimant has the right to say: my claim was in that one yard.
[PAGE_341]
Book of Sales. Commentary: The scenario is that a house is in the possession of one person; another person claims an unknown right in this house, and the possessor (i.e., the defendant) denies. Then the possessor compromises with the claimant for one hundred dirhams, i.e., the possessor gives one hundred dirhams to the claimant and takes his right. Then a third person establishes his entitlement to the entire house except one yard. Then the possessor (i.e., the defendant) will not have the option to take back any wealth from the claimant, because the claimant can say: my claim was in this one yard for which I took one hundred dirhams.
And if he claimed the entire house and made a compromise for one hundred dirhams, then a part of the house is claimed by someone else, then he will reclaim proportionally. He (the author) said: And if he claimed all of it, and compromised for one hundred dirhams, then something of it is found to belong to someone else, he reclaims proportionally to it, because reconciliation is impossible, so reclaiming its substitute is necessary when the safety of the exchanged object is lost. And this issue is evidence that compromise for an unknown thing for a known consideration is permissible, because ignorance (jahalah) in what is relinquished does not lead to dispute. Translation: And if the claimant claimed the entire house, then the possessor compromised with him for one hundred dirhams, then a portion of the house was claimed by someone else, then the possessor will take back from the claimant proportionally, because reconciliation is impossible. So reclaiming its substitute is obligatory when the safety of the exchanged object is lost. And this issue is evidence that compromise for an unknown thing for a known consideration is permissible, because ignorance in what is relinquished does not lead to dispute.
Explanation: The scenario is that one person has a house in his possession; another person claims the entire house. The possessor compromises with the claimant for one hundred dirhams, i.e., gives one hundred dirhams in exchange for the entire house. Then a third person establishes his entitlement to a part of the house, e.g., half, and takes it. In this case, the possessor (i.e., the defendant) will take back half of the one hundred dirhams from the claimant, i.e., fifty dirhams, because after the entitlement is proved, it becomes known that the claimant took these fifty dirhams without any right. The author of al-Hidayah says: This issue is evidence that compromise for an unknown thing for a known consideration is permissible, because ignorance in things that are relinquished does not create dispute, and ignorance that does not create dispute is not a cause for impermissibility, and this is the case here. Because when the claimant claimed an unknown right in the house and the possessor compromised with him for one hundred dirhams, it is as if the claimant relinquished his right in exchange for one hundred dirhams, and in matters of relinquishment (isqatat), ignorance does not lead to dispute. And ignorance that does not lead to dispute is also not a cause for impermissibility. Therefore, compromise for an unknown thing for a known consideration is permissible. Second, this issue indicates that the correctness of the claim is not a condition for the validity of the compromise (sulh), because the claim of an unknown right in the house is not valid due to ignorance, yet the compromise is valid. So it is known that the correctness of the claim is not a condition for the validity of the compromise. And Allah knows best. Jamil Ahmad, forgiven.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on two related rulings from al-Hidayah about compromise (sulh) after a claim of right in a house. The first scenario: the claimant claims an unknown right (haqq majhul) in a house, and the possessor (qabid) settles for 100 dirhams. Later, a third party proves that the entire house except one yard belongs to him. The possessor cannot recover any of the 100 dirhams from the claimant because the claimant can claim that his right was only in that remaining yard, and that portion belonged to him. The second scenario: the claimant claims the entire house, and the possessor settles for 100 dirhams. Later, a third party proves ownership of a portion (e.g., half) of the house. In this case, the possessor can recover proportionally (e.g., 50 dirhams) because the claimant received payment for the whole house, but only part of it was actually his. The commentary highlights two broader principles: (1) Sulh for an unknown (majhul) right in exchange for a known consideration is valid because the ignorance (jahalah) in what is being relinquished (isqat) does not lead to dispute (munaza‘ah). (2) The validity of sulh does not require the validity of the underlying claim (da‘wa); even if the claim is invalid due to ignorance, the sulh can still be valid. This is because sulh is a form of relinquishment (isqat), which is more flexible.

Key Terms
صلح (sulh): A compromise or settlement ending a dispute by mutual agreement, often involving payment.
مجہول (majhul): Unknown; here it refers to a right that is not specified (e.g., an unspecified share or claim).
استحقاق (istihqaq): Legal entitlement; a third party proving ownership of property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because the claimant had no right at all
B) Because the claimant can say his claim was in the remaining yard
C) Because the possessor agreed to pay without condition
D) Because the third party took the house

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits sulh for an unknown right, and once paid, the possessor cannot recover if the claimant can plausibly link his claim to the remaining portion.

Sulh for the Entire House: Proportional Recovery When Part is Lost
Explains the second scenario and the derived principle

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule of proportional recovery when a third party establishes ownership of part of a property after a sulh for the whole property, and explain the underlying principle that sulh for an unknown right is valid.

Core Idea
When sulh is for the entire house, recovery is proportional if part is taken by a third party.

This lesson analyzes the second scenario from al-Hidayah, where the claimant claims the entire house and settles for a known sum. Later, a third party proves ownership of a portion. Unlike the first scenario, the possessor can reclaim part of the payment. This ruling also serves as evidence for the broader Hanafi principle that sulh for an unknown right (majhul) is valid because ignorance in relinquishment does not lead to dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a sulh where the claimant claims the entire house and the possessor pays 100 dirhams to settle. A third party then proves ownership of a portion of the house. The ruling is that the possessor can recover from the claimant proportionally to the lost portion.
This is a different scenario from the first: here the claim is for the whole, not an unknown right.
2
Locate the dalil
The text provides a rational proof: 'because reconciliation is impossible, so reclaiming its substitute is necessary when the safety of the exchanged object is lost.' The sulh is treated like a sale: the payment was for the entire house, and since part is not owned by the claimant, the payment must be reduced proportionally.
The proof is a rational analogy to sales and istihsan.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The sulh for the entire house implies that the claimant transferred his alleged ownership of the whole. When part of that alleged ownership is invalidated by a third party, the consideration paid for that part must be returned. This is because the subject matter of the sulh is partially lost, so the price is adjusted.
This distinguishes from the first scenario where the claim was unspecified.
4
Note the ikhtilaf and the derived principle
The text uses this scenario to prove that sulh for an unknown right (majhul) is valid. The reasoning: ignorance in what is relinquished (isqat) does not lead to dispute, so jahalah does not invalidate the contract. Other schools may object to sulh for an unknown right due to gharar, but the Hanafi view is that isqat is more flexible.
This principle is a key Hanafi distinction.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The ruling is from al-Hidayah and is mufta bihi. The text explicitly states that this issue is evidence for the validity of sulh for majhul. The tarjih is based on the rational proof and the practice of the early Hanafi masters.
This is the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
کسی نے گھر میں مجہول حق کا دعوی کیا مدعی نے سو درہم پر صلح کر لی پھر گھر میں سوائے ایک گز کے کوئی مستحق نکل آیا تو قابض مدعی سے کچھ واپس نہیں لے سکتا قال ومن ادعى حقا في دار معناه حقا مجهولا فصالحه الذى فى يده على مائة درهم فاستحقت الدار الاذراعا منها لم يرجع بشتى لان للمدعى ان يقول دعواى في هذا الباقي ترجمہ اور اگر کسی نے ایک گھر میں کچھ حق کا دعوئی یعنی حق مجہول کا دعوی کیا پھر قابض نے مدعی سے سو درہم پر صلح کر لی پھر اس گھر میں سے علاوہ ایک گز کے مستحق ہو گیا تو قابض مدعی سے کچھ مال واپس نہیں لے سکتا ہے۔ کیونکہ مدعی کیلئے یہ کہنے کا حق ہے کہ میرا دعوئی اسی ایک گز میں تھا۔

كتاب البيوع
امداد
اشرف الہدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد ہشتم تشریح مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ ایک مکان ایک آدمی کے قبضہ میں ہے دوسرے آدمی نے اس مکان میں اپنے حق مجہول کا دعوی کیا اور قابض یعنی مدعلی علیہ نے انکار کیا پھر قابض نے مدعی سے ایک سو درہم پر صلح کی یعنی قابض نے ایک دوسو درہم مدعی کو دے کر اس کا حق مجبول لے لیا۔ پھر ایک تیسرے آدمی نے سوائے ایک گز کے اس پورے گھر پر اپنا استحقاق ثابت کر دیا تو قابض یعنی مدعی علیہ کو مدعی سے کچھ مال واپس لینے کا اختیار نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ مدعی یہ کہہ سکتا ہے کہ میرا دعومی اسی ایک گز میں تھا جس کے عوض میں نے ایک سو درہم لئے ہیں۔
اگر کل کا دعویٰ کیا اور سو درہم پر صلح کر لی پھر کسی چیز کا کوئی مستحق نکل آیا تو
اسی حساب سے رجوع کرے گا
وان ادعاها كلها فصالحه على مائة درهم فاستحق منها شيء رجع بحسابه لان التوفيق غير ممكن فوجب الرجوع ببدله عند قوات سلامة المبدل ودلت المسألة على ان الصلح عن المجهول على معلوم جائز لان الجهالة فيما يسقط لا تفضى الى المنازعة
ترجمہ اور اگر مدعی نے پورے گھر کا دعوی کیا پھر قابض نے سوار ہم پر اس سے صلح کر لی پھر گھر کا ایک حصہ مستحق ہو گیا تو قابض مدعی سے اسی کے حساب سے واپس لے گا۔ اسلئے کہ توفیق ناممکن ہے۔ پس سلامتی کمبدل کے فوت ہونے کے وقت اس کے بدل کا واپس لینا واجب ہوا۔ اور یہ مسئلہ اس بات پر دلیل ہے کہ مجہول چیز کے بارے میں مال معلوم پر صلح کرنا جائز ہے۔ اسلئے کہ جہالت اس چیز میں ساقط ہو جاتی ہے جو مفضی الی المنازعہ نہیں ہوتی ۔
تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ ایک آدمی کے قبضہ میں ایک مکان ہے دوسرے آدمی نے پورے مکان پر دعویٰ کیا پس قابض نے مدعی سے ایک سو درہم پر صلح کر لی یعنی پورے مکان کے عوض سو درہم دے دیئے۔ پھر اس مکان کے ایک حصہ پر کسی تیسرے آدمی نے اپنا استحقاق ثابت کر دیا مثلاً آدھا مکان استحقاق ثابت کر کے لے لیا تو اس صورت میں قابض یعنی مدعی علیہ، مدعی سے ایک سو درہم کا آدھا یعنی پچاس درہم واپس لے لے گا۔ کیونکہ استحقاق ثابت ہونے کے بعد معلوم ہو گیا کہ مدعی نے یہ پچاس درہم بغیر کسی حق کے لئے تھے۔ صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ یہ مسئلہ اس بات پر دلیل ہے کہ شی مجہول کے بارے میں مال معلوم پر صلح کرنا جائز ہے کیونکہ جہالت ان چیزوں میں جو ساقط ہو جاتی ہیں جھگڑا پیدا نہیں کرتی اور جو جہالت جھگڑا پیدا نہ کرے وہ عدم جواز کا سبب نہیں ہوتی اور یہاں یہی بات ہے۔ اسلئے کہ جب مدعی نے مکان میں حق مجہول کا دعوی کیا اور قابض نے اس سے ایک سو درہم پر صلح کر لی تو گویا مدعی نے ایک سو درہم کے عوض اپنا حق ساقط کر دیا ہے اور اسقاطات میں جہالت مفضی الی المنازعہ نہیں ہوتی اور جو جہالت مفضی الی المنازعہ نہ ہو وہ عدم جواز کا سب بھی نہیں ہوتی۔ اسلئے مال معلوم پر مجہول چیز کے بارے میں صلح کرنا بھی جائز ہوگا۔ دوسرے یہ مسئلہ اس بات پر دلالت کرتا ہے کہ صحت صلح کیلئے صحت دعوئی شرط نہیں ہے۔ کیونکہ مکان کے اندر حق مجہول کا دعوئی جہالت کی وجہ سے صحیح نہیں ہے۔ حالانکہ صلح صحیح ہے۔ پس معلوم ہوا کہ صحت صلح کیلئے صحت دعوئی شرط نہیں ہے۔ واللہ اعلم جمیل احمد غفرلہ
Someone claimed an unknown right in a house; the claimant made a compromise (sulh) for one hundred dirhams; then a third party became entitled to the entire house except one yard (dhira‘). The possessor cannot take anything back from the claimant. He (the author) said: And whoever claims a right in a house, meaning an unknown right, and the one in possession makes a compromise with him for one hundred dirhams, then the house is found to belong to someone else except a yard of it, he (the possessor) does not get back anything, because the claimant can say: my claim was in this remaining (yard). Translation: And if someone claims a right in a house, i.e., an unknown right, then the possessor compromises with the claimant for one hundred dirhams, then a third party establishes entitlement to the entire house except one yard, then the possessor cannot take back any wealth from the claimant, because the claimant has the right to say: my claim was in that one yard.
[PAGE_341]
Book of Sales. Commentary: The scenario is that a house is in the possession of one person; another person claims an unknown right in this house, and the possessor (i.e., the defendant) denies. Then the possessor compromises with the claimant for one hundred dirhams, i.e., the possessor gives one hundred dirhams to the claimant and takes his right. Then a third person establishes his entitlement to the entire house except one yard. Then the possessor (i.e., the defendant) will not have the option to take back any wealth from the claimant, because the claimant can say: my claim was in this one yard for which I took one hundred dirhams.
And if he claimed the entire house and made a compromise for one hundred dirhams, then a part of the house is claimed by someone else, then he will reclaim proportionally. He (the author) said: And if he claimed all of it, and compromised for one hundred dirhams, then something of it is found to belong to someone else, he reclaims proportionally to it, because reconciliation is impossible, so reclaiming its substitute is necessary when the safety of the exchanged object is lost. And this issue is evidence that compromise for an unknown thing for a known consideration is permissible, because ignorance (jahalah) in what is relinquished does not lead to dispute. Translation: And if the claimant claimed the entire house, then the possessor compromised with him for one hundred dirhams, then a portion of the house was claimed by someone else, then the possessor will take back from the claimant proportionally, because reconciliation is impossible. So reclaiming its substitute is obligatory when the safety of the exchanged object is lost. And this issue is evidence that compromise for an unknown thing for a known consideration is permissible, because ignorance in what is relinquished does not lead to dispute.
Explanation: The scenario is that one person has a house in his possession; another person claims the entire house. The possessor compromises with the claimant for one hundred dirhams, i.e., gives one hundred dirhams in exchange for the entire house. Then a third person establishes his entitlement to a part of the house, e.g., half, and takes it. In this case, the possessor (i.e., the defendant) will take back half of the one hundred dirhams from the claimant, i.e., fifty dirhams, because after the entitlement is proved, it becomes known that the claimant took these fifty dirhams without any right. The author of al-Hidayah says: This issue is evidence that compromise for an unknown thing for a known consideration is permissible, because ignorance in things that are relinquished does not create dispute, and ignorance that does not create dispute is not a cause for impermissibility, and this is the case here. Because when the claimant claimed an unknown right in the house and the possessor compromised with him for one hundred dirhams, it is as if the claimant relinquished his right in exchange for one hundred dirhams, and in matters of relinquishment (isqatat), ignorance does not lead to dispute. And ignorance that does not lead to dispute is also not a cause for impermissibility. Therefore, compromise for an unknown thing for a known consideration is permissible. Second, this issue indicates that the correctness of the claim is not a condition for the validity of the compromise (sulh), because the claim of an unknown right in the house is not valid due to ignorance, yet the compromise is valid. So it is known that the correctness of the claim is not a condition for the validity of the compromise. And Allah knows best. Jamil Ahmad, forgiven.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on two related rulings from al-Hidayah about compromise (sulh) after a claim of right in a house. The first scenario: the claimant claims an unknown right (haqq majhul) in a house, and the possessor (qabid) settles for 100 dirhams. Later, a third party proves that the entire house except one yard belongs to him. The possessor cannot recover any of the 100 dirhams from the claimant because the claimant can claim that his right was only in that remaining yard, and that portion belonged to him. The second scenario: the claimant claims the entire house, and the possessor settles for 100 dirhams. Later, a third party proves ownership of a portion (e.g., half) of the house. In this case, the possessor can recover proportionally (e.g., 50 dirhams) because the claimant received payment for the whole house, but only part of it was actually his. The commentary highlights two broader principles: (1) Sulh for an unknown (majhul) right in exchange for a known consideration is valid because the ignorance (jahalah) in what is being relinquished (isqat) does not lead to dispute (munaza‘ah). (2) The validity of sulh does not require the validity of the underlying claim (da‘wa); even if the claim is invalid due to ignorance, the sulh can still be valid. This is because sulh is a form of relinquishment (isqat), which is more flexible.

Key Terms
صلح (sulh): A compromise or settlement ending a dispute by mutual agreement, often involving payment.
مجہول (majhul): Unknown; here it refers to a right that is not specified (e.g., an unspecified share or claim).
استحقاق (istihqaq): Legal entitlement; a third party proving ownership of property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the second scenario, what is the evidence that sulh for an unknown right is valid?
A) The claimant could say his claim was in the remaining part
B) The text explicitly states that this issue is evidence
C) The possessor agreed to pay
D) A third party took part of the house

Key Takeaway
When sulh covers the entire property and a third party takes part, the possessor recovers proportionally. This case also proves the Hanafi rule that sulh for an unknown right is valid because ignorance in relinquishment does not cause dispute.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
10 min
Compromise (Sulh) over Unknown Right and Proportional Recovery upon Istihqaq
Explains two sulh scenarios and derives permissibility of sulh over unknown right

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on sulh over an unknown right and apply the proportional recovery rule when a third party proves ownership.

Core Idea
Sulh over unknown right is valid; proportional recovery applies when istihqaq occurs.

In the Book of Sales, al-Hidayah examines two scenarios where a claimant asserts an unknown right in property and the possessor compromises by paying a known amount. Later, a third party proves ownership over part or all of the property. The rulings differ based on whether the claim was for a specific portion or the whole. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and the underlying principle that ignorance in matters of dropping rights does not invalidate the compromise.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Read the two cases: (1) claimant claims an unknown right, possessor pays 100 dirhams, then third party proves ownership of all but one yard; (2) claimant claims the entire house, pays 100 dirhams, then third party proves half ownership.
The first scenario involves a limited claim (unknown right implying only a portion); the second involves a total claim.
2
Determine the ruling for each scenario
In scenario 1, no recovery for the possessor; in scenario 2, proportional recovery (half of the payment). Ask: why the difference?
The key is what the claimant's claim covered: in scenario 1, the claimant can say his unknown right was exactly the one yard; in scenario 2, the claim covered the whole, so the proven ownership retroactively reduces the consideration.
3
Extract the principle about jahalah in isqatat
The text states that 'ignorance in what is dropped does not lead to dispute.' This means that sulh over an unknown right for a known consideration is valid because the jahalah relates to dropping a right, not to an exchange that could cause conflict.
Contrast this with a sale, where ignorance of the subject matter invalidates the contract.
4
Understand the proof via istihsan and its implication
The dalil is istihsan: strict analogy would require the claim to be valid, but istihsan permits sulh because jahalah in isqatat is harmless. This also shows that a valid claim is not a condition for valid sulh.
The passage explicitly states: 'sihhat al-sulh li-ajl sihhat al-da'wa shartun laysa' (validity of claim is not a condition).

Book of Sales. Explanation: The case is that a house is in the possession of one person. Another person claimed an unknown right in that house, and the possessor (the defendant) denied it. Then the possessor made a compromise with the claimant for one hundred dirhams, i.e., the possessor gave one hundred dirhams to the claimant and took his unknown right. Then a third person proved his entitlement to the entire house except one yard. Then the possessor (the defendant) will have no right to take back any wealth from the claimant. Because the claimant can say: 'My claim was only for this one yard, in exchange for which I took one hundred dirhams.' If he claimed the entire house and made a compromise for one hundred dirhams, then something becomes entitled to someone else, he will reclaim in proportion. 'And if he claimed it all and made a compromise with him for one hundred dirhams, then part of it becomes entitled, he reclaims in proportion, because reconciliation is not possible, so it becomes obligatory to take back its substitute when the safety of the substitute is lost. And this issue indicates that compromise over the unknown for a known amount is permissible, because ignorance in what is dropped does not lead to dispute.' Because reconciliation is impossible. So when the safety of the substitute is lost, taking back its substitute becomes obligatory. And this issue is evidence that compromise over an unknown thing for known wealth is permissible. Because ignorance is dropped in that which does not lead to dispute. Explanation: The case is that a man has a house in his possession. Another man claimed the entire house. So the possessor made a compromise with the claimant for one hundred dirhams, i.e., he gave one hundred dirhams in exchange for the entire house. Then a third person proved his entitlement to a part of the house, for example he proved entitlement to half the house and took it. In that case, the possessor (the defendant) will take back half of the one hundred dirhams, i.e., fifty dirhams, from the claimant. Because after the entitlement is proven, it becomes known that the claimant took those fifty dirhams without any right. The author of Hidayah says: This issue indicates that compromise over an unknown thing for known wealth is permissible, because ignorance in things that are dropped does not create dispute, and ignorance that does not create dispute is not a cause for impermissibility. And this is the case here. Because when the claimant claimed an unknown right in the house and the possessor made a compromise with him for one hundred dirhams, it is as if the claimant dropped his right in exchange for one hundred dirhams, and in dropping (rights), ignorance does not lead to dispute, and ignorance that does not lead to dispute is not a cause for impermissibility. So compromise over an unknown thing for known wealth is permissible. Second, this issue indicates that the validity of the claim is not a condition for the validity of the compromise. Because the claim of an unknown right in the house is not valid due to ignorance, yet the compromise is valid. So it is known that the validity of the claim is not a condition for the validity of the compromise. And Allah knows best. Jamil Ahmad, may He forgive him.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling and proof discussion from the Book of Sales (Kitab al-Buyu) in Ashraf al-Hidayah. It addresses two scenarios of compromise (sulh) when a claimant asserts an unknown right (haqq majhul) in a property, and later a third party proves ownership (istihqaq) over part or all of that property. In the first scenario, the claimant claimed an unknown right in a house, and the possessor (defendant) compromised by giving 100 dirhams. Later, a third party proved ownership of the entire house except one yard. The ruling: the possessor cannot reclaim any money from the claimant, because the claimant can argue that his unknown right was precisely that one yard, for which the 100 dirhams were a fair exchange. The rationale is that the possessor voluntarily paid for the unknown claim, and the subsequent istihqaq does not retroactively invalidate the sulh since the claim was limited. In the second scenario, the claimant claimed the entire house, and the possessor compromised for 100 dirhams. Then a third party proved ownership of half the house. Here, the possessor can reclaim half (50 dirhams) proportionally. The reason: the sulh covers the entire house, but when half is proven to belong to another, that half effectively was not owned by the claimant, so the payment for that portion was without basis. The text then draws two important principles: (1) Sulh over an unknown right (majhul) for a known consideration is valid (ja'iz). This is because ignorance (jahalah) in matters of dropping rights (isqatat) does not lead to dispute (la tufdi ila al-niza'), unlike ignorance in exchange contracts. Therefore, such sulh is permissible. (2) The validity of the underlying claim is not a condition for the validity of the sulh. Even if the claim of an unknown right is itself invalid due to jahalah, the sulh is still valid. This shows that sulh is a separate contract that does not require a valid antecedent claim. The commentary follows the matn of al-Hidayah and explains the proofs: the impossibility of reconciling the conflicting claims (tawfiq ghayr mumkin) necessitates proportional recovery, and the general permissibility of sulh over majhul is derived from the principle that jahalah in isqatat does not cause harm.

Key Terms
صلح (sulh): A contract of compromise to settle a claim or dispute, often involving the payment of consideration to drop a right.
مجهول (majhul): In this context, an unspecified or unknown right claimed against property.
استحقاق (istihqaq): The proven entitlement or ownership of a third party over part of the disputed property.
اسقاط (isqat): Dropping of a right; in this context, the claimant drops his claim in exchange for payment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the passage, what is the basis for the permissibility of sulh over an unknown right?
A) The claim must be valid for sulh to be valid.
B) Ignorance in matters of dropping rights does not lead to dispute.
C) The Hanafi school allows all unknown rights in contracts.
D) The possessor's payment makes the unknown right known.

Key Takeaway
Sulh over an unknown right for a known consideration is valid in the Hanafi school because jahalah in isqatat does not lead to dispute; proportional recovery applies only when the claim covered the entire property and part is proven to belong to another.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
