Partial Ownership: Entitlement to Penalty vs. Emancipation
Explains Imam Muhammad's ruling on the degree of ownership needed for penalty vs emancipation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to DISTINGUISH between the requirement of full ownership for emancipation and partial ownership for penalty entitlement in Hanafi law, and apply the underlying principle to analogous cases.

Core Idea
Partial ownership (min wajh malik) suffices for penalty but not for emancipation—complete ownership is required.

In this passage, Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani addresses a subtle but important distinction in Hanafi property law: the degree of ownership required for different legal acts. While entitlement to a penalty for injury to a slave can be established through partial ownership, the act of emancipation demands full and complete ownership. This distinction is demonstrated through analogies with the mukatab and the buyer with khiyar al-shart.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
Read the passage and identify the two distinct rulings: entitlement to a penalty (jurmanah) and emancipation (i'taq). Note that the same person (partial owner) is treated differently for each.
2
Extract the legal principle
The passage states that for penalty, 'partial ownership is sufficient' (min wajh malik kafi), while for emancipation, 'complete ownership is necessary' (mulk kamil zaruri). State this principle clearly.
3
Analyze the first analogy (mukatab)
Consider the mukatab (a slave under kitabah) who receives a penalty for injury but later reverts to slavery. The penalty goes to the master because the mukatab's ownership was only partial. Identify the asl (mukatab's partial ownership) and the hukm (penalty belongs to master).
This establishes that partial ownership is enough to receive penalty (though the beneficiary is the master, not the mukatab himself).
4
Analyze the second analogy (buyer with khiyar al-shart)
A buyer with the option of condition (khiyar al-shart) has partial ownership during the option period. If the slave is injured, the buyer is entitled to the penalty. Compare with the mukatab case: both have partial ownership.
The illa (effective cause) for both is partial ownership.
5
Contrast with emancipation by buyer from usurper
The buyer from a usurper also has only partial ownership. According to Imam Muhammad, his emancipation is invalid because emancipation requires complete ownership. Apply the principle: partial ownership is insufficient for emancipation.
This completes the distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
امام محمد کی طرف سے جواب یہ ہے کہ جرمانہ کا مستحق ہونے کیلئے من وجہ مالک ہونا کافی ہے کامل ملک کا حاصل ہونا ضروری نہیں ہے جیسے مکاتب کا ہاتھ کاٹا گیا اور اس نے جرمانہ لے لیا پھر وہ عاجز ہو کر رقیق ہو گیا تو یہ مال اسکے مولی کا ہوتا ہے اور جیسے مشتری کے قبضہ میں مبیع یعنی غلام کا ہاتھ کاٹا گیا اور بائع کیلئے خیار شرط تھا۔ پھر بیع کی اجازت دیدی گئی تو اس صورت میں بھی چونکہ مشتری کیلئے من وجہ ملک حاصل ہے۔ اسلئے جرمانہ کا مال اس کیلئے ہو گا اس کے برخلاف مشتری من الغاصب کا آزاد کرنا تو امام محمد کے نزدیک وہ نافذ نہ ہوگا کیونکہ آزاد کرنے کیلئے ملک کامل کا ہونا ضروری ہے من وجہ ملک کافی نہیں ہوتی ہے اور مشتری من الغاصب کیلئے چونکہ من وجہ ملک ثابت ہوتی ہے لئے مشترى من الغاصب کا آزاد کرنا درست نہ ہوگا۔
From Imam Muhammad, the answer is that for entitlement to the penalty, being a partial owner is sufficient; complete ownership is not necessary. For example, a mukatab's hand is cut, and he takes the penalty, then he becomes unable and returns to slavery, so that wealth belongs to his master. And similarly, a slave (sold) in the buyer's possession has his hand cut, and the seller had the option of condition, then the sale was permitted; in that case also, since the buyer has partial ownership, the penalty wealth is for him. Contrary to the emancipation done by the buyer from the usurper, in Imam Muhammad's view it is not effective, because for emancipation complete ownership is necessary; partial ownership is not sufficient. And since the buyer from the usurper has only partial ownership, his emancipation is not valid.
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage presents Imam Muhammad's ruling on two related issues: entitlement to a penalty (jurmanah) and emancipation (i'taq). The key principle is that partial ownership (min wajh malik) is sufficient for claiming a penalty, but complete ownership (mulk kamil) is required for emancipation. The author provides two examples: a mukatab (a slave under contract of manumission) who receives a penalty for injury but then fails to pay and reverts to slavery; the penalty belongs to the master because the mukatab's ownership was partial. Similarly, a buyer with khiyar al-shart (option of condition) owns the slave partially; if the slave is injured, the buyer gets the penalty. In contrast, emancipation by a buyer from a usurper (mushtari min al-ghasib) is invalid because his ownership is only partial, and emancipation requires complete ownership.

Key Terms
من وجہ ملک (min wajh malik): Partial ownership, where the owner has some proprietary interest but not full and absolute title.
جرمانہ (jurmanah): A penalty or compensation paid for injury to a slave.
مکاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered into a contract of manumission (kitabah) with his master, agreeing to pay a sum in installments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Muhammad, why is the emancipation of a slave by a buyer from a usurper (mushtari min al-ghasib) invalid?
A) Because the buyer did not have physical possession.
B) Because the buyer has only partial ownership (min wajh malik), and emancipation requires complete ownership.
C) Because the usurper (ghasib) is the true owner.
D) Because the slave was not in the buyer's possession at the time of emancipation.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the degree of ownership determines the validity of legal acts: partial ownership suffices for receiving compensation, but full ownership is required for manumission.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

6 min
Second Sale Invalid After Permission of First
Ruling on second sale validity after owner's permission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on a second sale by a buyer with suspended ownership, explain the reasoning based on gharar, and distinguish the exceptional validity of emancipation.

Core Idea
A second sale by a buyer with suspended ownership is invalid due to gharar, but emancipation is valid because gharar does not affect acts of worship.

Imagine someone buys a slave from a usurper (ghasib) without the owner's permission. Before the owner confirms the sale, the buyer resells the slave to a third party. Later, the owner permits the first sale. Is the second sale valid? The Hanafi school says no. This lesson unpacks the two reasons for this ruling and the important exception for emancipation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and transactions
List the actors: owner (mawla), usurper (ghasib), first buyer (mushtari min al-ghasib), and second buyer. The first sale is suspended (mawquf) on owner's permission; the second sale occurs before permission.
The first buyer holds a suspended ownership (mulk mawquf).
2
Understand the first reason: absolute ownership overrides suspended ownership
When the owner permits the first sale, the first buyer acquires absolute ownership (mulk qati') retroactively. This absolute ownership nullifies the suspended ownership that existed at the time of the second sale, making the second sale void ab initio.
This is a rule of property law: a stronger right extinguishes a weaker one.
3
Recognize the second reason: gharar (uncertainty)
The second sale's validity depends on an uncertain event—whether the owner will permit the first sale. This dependency creates gharar (risk of dissolution), which invalidates a sale contract according to Hanafi law.
The passage uses the term 'infisakh' (dissolution) to describe the risk.
4
Contrast with emancipation
According to Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani), the first buyer's emancipation of the slave is valid even before permission, despite similar uncertainty. The reason is that gharar does not affect acts of worship (ibadah); the prohibition of gharar is specific to commercial contracts.
This distinction is supported by analogy: a sale before possession is invalid due to gharar, but emancipation before possession is valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مشتری نے غلام دوسرے کو بیچ دیا پھر مولیٰ نے بیع اول کی اجازت دیدی تو بیع ثانی جائز نہیں
قال فإن باعه المشترى من اخر ثم اجاز المولى البيع الأول لم يجز البيع الثاني لما ذكرنا و لان فيه غرر الانفساخ على اعتبار عدم الاجازة فى البيع الاول والبيع يفسد به بخلاف الاعتاق عندهما لانه لا يوثر فيه الغرر
ترجمہ پھر اگر مشتری من الغاصب نے یہ غلام کسی دوسرے کے ہاتھ فروخت کر دیا۔ پھر مولی نے بیج اول کی اجازت دے دی، تو بیچ ثانی جائز نہیں ہے اس دلیل کی وجہ سے جو ہم نے ذکر کی ہے اور اسلئے کہ بیع اول میں عدم اجازت کا اعتبار کرتے ہوئے بیع ثانی میں عقد منح ہونے کا دھوکہ ہے۔ حالانکہ ایسے دھوکہ سے بیع فاسد ہو جاتی ہے۔ برخلاف سیخین کے نزدیک اعتاق جائز ہونے کے کیونکہ اعتاق میں دھو کہ موثر نہیں ہوتا ہے۔
تشریح مسلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر مشتری من الغاصب نے یہ غلام کسی دوسر آدمی کے ہاتھ فروخت کیا۔ پھر مولی نے بیع اول کی اجازت دیدی تو بیج ثانی جائز نہ ہوگی۔ ایک دلیل تو سابق میں گذر چکی کہ جب ملک قطعی ، ملک موقوف پر طاری ہوتی ہے تو وہ ملک موقوف کو باطل کر دیتی ہے۔ دوسری دلیل یہ ہے کہ بیع ثانی کا نافذ ہونا اس امر پر موقوف ہے کہ بیع اول کی اجازت حاصل ہو۔ لیکن احتمال ہے کہ مولی بیج اول کی اجازت نہ دے پس بیچ ثانی میں دھوکہ ہے اور جس بیچ میں ایسا دھوکہ ہو وہ فاسد ہوتی ہے اگر آپ یہ کہیں کہ جس طرح مشتری من الغاصب کا بیچ کرنا جائز نہیں ہے ویسے ہی اس کا آزاد کرنا بھی جائز نہ ہونا چاہئے ۔ حالانکہ یخین کے نزدیک آزاد کرنا جائز ہے۔ اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ یہاں صرف دھو کہ ہے اور دھو کہ بیع کو فاسد کرتا ہے اور رہا اعتماق تو اس کو ایسا دھوکہ فاسد نہیں کرتا اسلئے اعتماق جائز ہے اور بیع جائز نہیں ہے جیسے منقولات میں قبضہ کرنے سے پہلے بیچ فسخ کے دھوکہ کی وجہ سے ناجائز ہوتی ہے مگر قبضہ کرنے سے پہلے اعتاق جائز ہے۔
If the buyer sells the slave to another, then the owner permits the first sale, the second sale is not valid. He (al-Hidayah author) said: For if the buyer sold it to another, then the owner permitted the first sale, the second sale is not valid, for the reason we mentioned, and because there is uncertainty (gharar) of dissolution (infisakh) considering the possibility of non-permission of the first sale, and a sale is invalidated by that. Contrary to emancipation according to the Two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), because gharar does not affect it. Translation and explanation follow: The second sale is invalid because absolute ownership terminates the suspended ownership. Also, the second sale depends on permission of the first; if permission is denied, the second sale fails, creating gharar. Emancipation is valid because gharar does not affect emancipation, just as in movables, a sale before possession is invalid due to gharar of dissolution, but emancipation before possession is valid.
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage deals with a situation where the buyer from a usurper (mushtari min al-ghasib) sells the slave to a third party before the owner permits the first sale. Later, the owner permits the first sale. The ruling is that the second sale is invalid (ghayr ja'iz). Two reasons are given: (1) When the owner permits the first sale, the buyer's absolute ownership (mulk qati') from that permission retroactively terminates his suspended ownership (mulk mawquf), so the second sale is void because the buyer had no effective title at the time of the second sale. (2) The second sale involves gharar (uncertainty) because its validity depends on whether the owner will permit the first sale; this uncertainty renders the sale fasid (voidable). The passage contrasts with emancipation (i'taq): according to Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), if the buyer emancipates the slave, that emancipation is valid even before permission, because gharar does not affect emancipation—it is an act of worship that is not invalidated by uncertainty. The analogy is made to the rule that a sale of movable property before taking possession is invalid due to gharar, but emancipation before possession is valid.

Key Terms
غرر (gharar): Uncertainty or risk that invalidates a contract; here the uncertainty is whether the owner will permit the first sale, making the second sale contingent.
ملک موقوف (mulk mawquf): Suspended ownership - an ownership that is effective only upon fulfillment of a condition, here the owner's permission.
انفساخ (infisakh): Dissolution or cancellation of a contract; here, the possibility that the first sale might be dissolved if permission is not granted.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a second sale of a slave by a buyer from a usurper invalid even after the owner permits the first sale?
A) Because the second sale is made without the owner's consent.
B) Because it involves gharar (uncertainty) as the second sale's validity depends on uncertain permission of the first sale.
C) Because the buyer never had possession of the slave.
D) Because the slave is not owned by the buyer at the time of the second sale.

Key Takeaway
A sale with gharar is invalid; this principle overrides any subsequent ratification, while emancipation remains valid as an act of worship.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Permission Requires Extant Subject Matter
Ruling on permission after subject matter perishes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for valid permission in a suspended sale and apply it to cases where the subject matter perishes before permission.

Core Idea
Permission cannot revive a sale if the subject matter no longer exists.

A suspended sale (al-bay' al-mawquf) depends on the owner's subsequent permission. But what if the item is destroyed before that permission? This passage clarifies that permission is only meaningful if the item still exists. The key is that the subject matter (ma'qud 'alayh) must be present at the time of permission, and a dead or destroyed item cannot be replaced by a substitute because the buyer's ownership was never fully established.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the ruling
Read the passage to find the case: a buyer from a usurper (al-mushtarī min al-ghāṣib) does not resell the slave, but the slave dies or is killed in the buyer's possession. Then the owner gives permission. The ruling is that the sale is not valid (lā yajūz).
The sale remains invalid (bāṭil) — permission does not cure the defect.
2
Locate the condition for valid permission
The text states: 'al-ijāzah min sharthā qiyām al-maʻqūd ʻalayh' — permission requires the subject matter to be extant. This is the shart (condition).
The condition must be met at the time of permission, not at the time of the original sale.
3
Understand why a substitute (badal) does not help
The passage explains that in a valid sale, if the item is destroyed, the buyer can claim a substitute (e.g., price or compensation) because ownership is established. Here, the buyer's ownership was suspended (mawqūf) and never became absolute, so no substitute can be imposed. Thus, the item is considered completely lost (fāta).
The contrast with a valid sale highlights the difference: established ownership vs. suspended ownership.
4
Apply the ruling to a new scenario
Suppose someone buys a car from a person who does not own it (fudūlī). The owner later gives permission, but by then the car is totaled. According to this ruling, the permission does not validate the sale because the car no longer exists.
The ruling is general: the subject matter must exist at permission, regardless of how it was lost.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اگر مشتری نے نہیں بیچا اور اسکے ہاتھ میں مر گیا یا قتل کر دیا گیا پھر بیع کو جائز قرار دیا تو بیع جائز نہ ہوگی
قال فان لم يبعه المشترى فمات فى يده او قتل ثم اجاز البيع لم يجز لما ذكرنا ان الاجازة من شرطها قيام المعقود عليه وقد فات بالموت وكذا بالقتل اذ لا يمكن ايجاب البدل للمشترى باقل حتى يعد باقيا ببقاء البدل لانه لا ملك للمشترى عند القتل ملكا يقابل بالبدل فيتحقق الفوات بخلاف البيع الصحيح لان ملک المشتری ثابت فامكن ايجاب البدل له فيكون المبيع قائما بقيام خلفه
ترجمہ اور اگر مشتری من الغاصب نے اس غلام مبیع کو فروخت نہیں کیا لیکن وہ مشتری کے قبضہ میں مر گیا یا قتل کیا گیا پھر مالک نے بیع کی اجازت دے دی تو یہ بیع جائز نہ ہوگی۔ اس دلیل کی وجہ سے جو ہم نے ذکر کی کہ اجازت کی شرط معقود علیہ کا موجود ہونا ہے۔ حالانکہ موت اور ایسے ہی قتل کی وجہ سے معقود علیہ فوت ہو گیا اسلئے کہ قتل کی وجہ سے مشتری کیلئے قیمت واجب کرنا ممکن نہیں ہے۔ تا کہ بدل موجود ہونے سے معقود علیہ موجود شمار کیا جائے۔ اسلئے کہ قتل کے وقت مشتری کیلئے ایسی ملک نہ تھی جس کے مقابلے میں عوض ہو پس فوت ہونا متفق ہوگا۔ برخلاف بیع صحیح کے۔ اسلئے کہ ملک مشتری ثابت ہے پس اس کے لئے بدل کا واجب کرنا ممکن ہے۔ پس اس کے خلیفہ کے موجود ہونے سے مبیع موجود ہوگی۔
And if the buyer did not sell it, but it died in his hand or was killed, then the owner gives permission for the sale, the sale is not valid, for the reason we mentioned: that permission requires the subject matter to be extant, and it has perished by death, and likewise by killing, because it is not possible to make a substitute (badal) obligatory for the buyer such that the item is considered extant by the existence of the substitute, because at the time of killing, the buyer does not have an ownership that can be compensated by a substitute. Thus, the loss is realized. Unlike a valid sale, because the buyer's ownership is established, so a substitute can be made obligatory for him, and thus the sold item is considered extant by the existence of its substitute.
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage discusses a scenario where the buyer from a usurper does not resell the slave, but the slave dies or is killed while in the buyer's possession. Later, the owner gives permission for the original sale. The ruling is that the sale does not become valid (ghayr ja'iz). The reason is that permission (ijazah) requires the subject matter (ma'qud 'alayh) to be in existence at the time of permission. Since the slave is dead or killed, it no longer exists. Furthermore, unlike a valid sale where the buyer has established ownership and can claim a substitute (such as the price or compensation from the killer), here the buyer's ownership was suspended (mawquf) and thus not strong enough to be compensated by a substitute. Therefore, the subject matter is considered completely lost, and permission cannot revive the sale. The passage contrasts this with a valid sale: if a buyer in a valid sale takes possession and the item is destroyed, the buyer has a right to a substitute (like the price or damages) and the sale remains valid because the substitute stands in for the item.

Key Terms
معقود علیہ (ma'qud 'alayh): The subject matter of the contract, i.e., the item being sold; here the slave.
بدل (badal): Substitute or compensation; here, the price or monetary equivalent that could stand in for the lost item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the owner's permission not validate the sale if the slave dies before permission?
A) Because permission must be given before the contract is made.
B) Because the subject matter (ma'qud 'alayh) no longer exists at the time of permission, and the buyer's suspended ownership cannot be compensated.
C) Because the buyer did not have possession of the slave.
D) Because the slave's death was caused by the buyer.

Key Takeaway
Permission for a suspended sale is only effective if the subject matter still exists at the time of permission; if it is destroyed, the contract remains invalid.
